Our Trada is Big 1 Our Store is Big !
Our Ideas are Bi gl\
We get bigger every day; on Saturday night
we are bigger than on the previous Monday,
and on the Wednesday following we are bigger
still. We can’t help growing. We have all the
elements of growth. The soil we are planted in
is good, the climate propitious, the people kind.
We are continually watered by the lifegiving
showers of a nation’s approval. Can anyone
wonder that we grow ?
Our growth is a wonderful thing. People
describe it as magical. There is no magic in it.
Anyone, or any set of people, can do the same if
they do as we have done. But they don’t; they
can’t find the way, charge too much for the privi-
lege of doing business with them, and generally
and deplorably make a mess of the whole affair.
That’s the mischief.
Most People Like XT’s! All Believe in Us!
Not % Pew Swear by Us I
It’s cupboard love. We suit them, make their
money go a long way, and generally render the
path of life an easy one. There’s no sentiment
about it. It’s real biz, and no mistake. In that
way we are beloved throughout Australia, the
measure of affection each one bestows upon us
being gauged by the amount of benefit received.
The more you buy at our store the greater your
liking for us will become, because the more you
invest with us the better dividends you receive.

Dear Bulletin, — Thanks to our social
system every Australian town is more or less
pestered with loafers and thieves. Some especially
vile scoundrels live in Nyngan (N.S.W.). When-
ever a traveller who possesses a good-looking
sheep-dog comes to that town, these. cigarette-
ft! 10 Milliard-sharps watch his camp, and at
the first opportunity the dog is caught (sheep-
dogs are mostly good-tempered) and tied up in
some back-yard until the unfortunate swagman is
either tired of looking for him, or, more fre-
quently, can’t afford to camp any longer. No
sooner lias he cleared than these mean creatures
sell his dog to passing drovers. This trade
nourishes in Nyngan—no less than three travellers
having lost dogs there within the last fortnight.
I once. But where is the policeman who would
stir his feet or take his fab hands out of his
pockets to help a swagman to recover his dog—
W J?° *?> P er haps, his only mate, his sole object of
affection on the lonely track, and more, perhaps
his only means of earning an honest crust; for a
man without a dog is seldom employed by men in
charge of travelling-stock.— 6xB. [The- Bulle-
tin does not think quite so meanly of the police.]

Dear Bulletin, — In your “Answers to
Correspondents, of a recent issue, you make some
remarks about the impossibility of independence
m running a country paper, and I want to make
some statements in rebuttal. It is possible to be
independent in any country town, but it is not pos-
sible to be successful—and it is possible to be
slavish and orawlsome in some country towns and
not be successful either. I have been both, and I
ought to know. My independence was always
genuine, but my alleged subscribers may have
seen that my crawlsorneness was a fraud. At
any rate, I wasn’t successful either way. During a
fit of temporary insanity I started a paper at a
goat hamlet in Gippsland, near Wilson’s Pro-
montory. I began with a circulation of 250, and
a free-list of 22. At the end of six months I
found I had really had a free-list of 278, and not
one subscriber. I also found that most of the
advertisers were on the free-list, also, and those
who were ready to liquidate my claims insisted, if
they were storekeepers, that I should take it out in
shoddy clothes and tinned fish, and, if publicans,
in the usual press manner. I have seen beauti-
fully iridescent macaws eating rainbow - snakes
for quite two days after collecting an account of
this kind. The hamlet and the public goat (which
was evidently the raison d’etre for settlement, as
the nannies seemed to come along first and be
merely followed by their owners) were owned by
one man. He could stand on a cleared bill above
the town, and all round as far as he could see the
dirt was his. lam not sure Whether he hadn’t
the fee simple of the atmosphere as well. Well,
I never mentioned him in my rag except to point
him as a moral for a sermon on land-nationalisa-
tion, and yet first time I wanted money he lent
me all I asked for. The money I borrowed from
him and repaid was about the only local coin re-
ceived during my six months’ ownership of the
paper. Ihe church would send in an order for
printing and advertising some tea-fight, and when
I mildly asked for payment a month after the
parson said that the Lord was a dead head, or
words to that effect. And it rained. Great
Scott ! how it rained. For nine months of the
year the place was ankle-deep in mud, and in
coming away from the alb-ged newspaper office on
publishing nights I used to fall over kangaroos
I and stumps and other features of the primeval
I bush. The full force of the Southern ocean would
curl over the promontory and strike the hamlet
right in the teeth, and keep on striking it for about
three weeks on end. In the summer the place
was full of snakes, and you couldn’t safely drink
anything but soda and lime-juice. The next-door
neighbor was a bootmaker and an advertiser, and
I didn’t like to object when his sticky infants
removed 51bs. of brevier quads so as they could
be amused by the quads melting in the fire. There
was also a loose-mouthed person who spent his
life in stretching cows’ udders and carting the
product to the railway, and his name sounded
like an indecent offence. This person used to put
some of his mouth into the office and say, “That’s
fine po’try yah put in thish yer paper. Did yer
write it outer your ’ead?” Furthermore, one of
my apprentices was the son of a local tin god
who had darkly hinted at paying me a premium
shortly. His son was the worst sort of a self-
soddened jay, but I thought of the premium and
bore it patiently. He used to shoot native bears
who must have been more than usually idiotic,
as they never attempted to chase him, and he
would skin the stinking things on my table and
nail the hides out to dry on the floor, so that the
unthinking visitor caught his large native feet in
the tacks and fell flop on the grease and blood-
stains. No premium eventuated, only three or
four people paid their accounts, and I was
frozen out, and a Scotch person took it over. I
would have starved but for Government advts.
—the indirect provincial press subsidy which
keeps alive many of the small country strugglers
in Australian journalism. A country newspaper
is an idyllic life for any nice, quiet grandfather
who objects to noise, but I have never been able
to calmly contemplate provincial journalism since
an advertising ironmonger offered to settle his bill
in rat-traps.—Randolph the Reckless.

while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
19 of this issue.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note,' of stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every, week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney . In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and, acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
' communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire thoie sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Blade and White.
Anyone Supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, SI Fleet Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal -.—The Standard and Diggers
News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also: — R. A. Thompson and Co., 3 Church Street ,
Capetown, South Africa.
Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, Queensland,
p.nd South Australia.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage by the Australian and Tasmanian Governments
on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual subscription
outside N.S, Wales is now £l, with 3s. 3d, added for
pottage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

Coloured wrapper denotes expiration of subscription.

SATURDAY, MARCH 30, 1895.
A Word to the Judges.
The banditti of the Pyrenees are said to
prey upon the traveller by hereditary right.
Immemorial prescription calls the sons of
every family to be professors of the gentle
art of snatching purses ; and it is not uncom-
mon to see three generations of brigands
together plundering one’s baggage with the
greatest unanimity. The father takes your
coat; the son your breeches; and thegay grand-
sire courteously demands the senor s shirt.
When at last you stand naked and shivering,
you have the consolation of knowing that, at
all events, the booty has been kept in the
family, and that no interloping stranger is a
peseta richer for your loss. The victim of
Australian lawyers is likely to have precisely
the same consolation. In some provinces the
disentanglement of law, the dispensation of
justice, are rapidly becoming a mere family
affair. If, in a spasm of anger or folly, a
man crosses a solicitor’s threshold, and after-
wards takes his case to Court, he very likely
notices that the barrister feed on his behalf
possesses the same characteristic lineaments
as the attorney who took his statement and
his guineas. Beneath the bench he sees
those lineaments budding on the face of
adolescence. Perplexed, he glances at the
Judge who is to decide the cause, and there
again are the family traits, accentuated by
the passage of many years. He wins his
suit. “Ah,” says an acquaintance, “you
employed Cheek and Jowl : you were
wise.” But the loser, who employed
Brow and Beak? Well, he knows,
like the Pyreneean traveller, that his
money has gone to support none of your
new-fangled harpies, but an ancient and re-
spectable family. Besides, Brow and Beak
were off their midden. See them in
Inequity, where Beak, senior, presides !
# * *
This monopoly of the practice and profits
of law by a few fortunate families is a grave
scandal and a growing danger to the Austra-
lian administration of justice. The number
of seats at the Feast of Clients is strictly
limited, and, bit by bit, the hungry ones who
have not the wedding-garment of kinship
with a Judge are being pushed to the door.
The Judge is invariably the root of the evil.
Under the shield of his authority the
members of his family stand back to back,
acting as one man for aggression or defence.
Against their united influence an outsider
usually fights in vain. If a clever man
resists, he may be brought into the charmed
circle by the power of the distaff, to become
a fresh source of strength and to create a
fresh weakness in the side of opponents.
Should he refuse alliance, war may be
declared, and nine times in ten the clever
man succumbs to odds and goes under. If
he prove too strong, the weight of the Judge
is in reserve to be consciously or uncon-
sciously thrown into the scale, and the issue
of the struggle is sure.
• # «
The risk and loss which the public incur
under such circumstances are sufficiently
obvious. With confidence in the direction of
justice once lost (even by the most brutal and
ignorant) the virtue is gone out of any com-
munity. Already there is uneasiness, doubt,
suspicion. Whether that suspicion is well
or ill-founded does not in the least affect the
question : it exists, and should be removed
in the only way possible, by removing its
cause. “ What !” say the friends and rela-
tives of the Judges concerned, “do you
accuse our father, our brother, our uncle,
our father-in-law, our patron, of bias 1”
The Bulletin does not, although it believes
that no man can conduct cases or give judg-
ments in which the success or welfare of
members of his family is intimately con-
cerned, without being consciously or uncon-
sciously biased. But the point is that,
whether the bias exists or not, the public
and a large section of the legal profession
believe it to exist, and the effect is to destroy
that faith in the purity of justice which is
indispensable to its satisfactory administra-
tion. For example, The Bulletin would
never say that any Australian Judge is impro-
perly influenced when his son or his son-in-law
or his nephew pleads before him. But a suitor
who recently lost his case before a Judge
whose relative was opposing counsel, signifi-
cantly remarked that “he did not believe
there was a man alive who could fight a
Judge and a Judge’s relative at one and the
same time.” The Judge, of course, knows
(and The Bulletin knows) that he decided
the case upon its merits and according to the
evidence, and that there was no relative in
court for him ; but evidently the suitor dis-
agrees. That the suitor should have a
specious pretext for disagreeing is a public
calamity.
• # *
And the worst of it is that, in such cases,
so many suitors disagree. Many people,
for example, believed that the surpris-
ing practice formerly enjoyed by the son
of an Australian ex-judge in his father’s
court was due chiefly to the relationship of
the parties. The Judge and his son (and
The Bulletin) were aware, of course, that
this was an error ; but, nevertheless, Parlia-
ment was supplied with a return which
showed that, in cases heard before his father,
the son, though a comparatively junior bar-
rister, held far more briefs than the oldest and
ablest other counsel practising. It is notice-
able, also, that while those of this ex-Judge’s
sons who started business when their father
was firmly planted on the Bench, are
reputed to have done extremely well; those
other who started shortly previous to his
retirement are reputed to have done—not so
well. And the foolish public—wrongly, of
course —persists in drawing inferences from
these things.
* # *
Unluckily, the public never will refrain
from drawing inferences. When, some time
ago, in an Australian Supreme Court case,
the Judge who opened the sittings was
unable to proceed, and his place was taken
by another Judge ; a near relative of this
second Judge, who had not previously been
briefed in an important case listed, was
immediately given a brief for one of the
parties, and some low-minded people con-
cerned in the case immediately began to
draw inferences. The Judge and his relative
(and The Bulletin) know, of course, that
the inferences are quite unfounded ; but
the mouth of the Judge is shut, and his
relative has little opportunity of con-
vincing the pig-headed public of its error.
So that, apparently, there are only four
ways of stopping these inferences, which are
calculated so seriously to prejudice the ad-
ministration of justice. The first is to pro-
vide that Judges shall have no relatives what-
ever. This would be difficult. A way might
be found of ensuring that Judges, like priests,
should never marry, or that if they did
marry they should have no children ; but this
would not protect the country from brothers,
or from nephews, or from the persons who
would lay siege to the affections of the Judge’s
maiden aunts, witli the base motive of be-
coming his relation by marriage. Besides,
to find a Judge absolutely without kith or
kin is impossible, unless a new race of law-
yers could be specially hatched from eggs
made of Blackstone and protoplasm, without
the intervention of father and mother.
Therefore, this way does not seem feasible.
* • *
Nor does the suggestion that Judges should
have enough relations to supply all the
demands of both parties bear close inspec-
tion. At first blush there is fascination in
the idea that every Judge should have two
sons practising before him, or four, or six, or
sixteen. If he went as high as twenty-two
(and some Australian Judges have not been
unworthy to stand before Beranger’s ‘ 1 bon
petit roi,” who had a hundred good reasons
to be called the father of his people), then
suitors could pick a team of eleven barristers
a-side, tossing for first pick, and have the
old man as umpire. When a Judge has only
one son, as sometimes happens at present,
the side which snaps up that son often con-
siders that it has an advantage. Fora Judge
is not a calculating-machine running without
friction, and producing definite results from
given factors. He is a human being, with
human appetites, habits, whims and pre-
judices, moved by human motives, liable to
human ailments. Consider how dexterously
a clever son, a nephew, or son-in-law may
manage him to gain his client’s ends ! And
the relative is the only man who has an authen-
tic clue to his Honor’s character. Other
advocates are like musicians set before a
piano where all the keys are jumbled, fo
that a key which, by position, should sound
C, actually sounds G, and G sounds E, while
E sounds a note two octaves higher. But
the son (say) has practised on the piano
since infancy, and can make it play any tune
he wishes. He knows his father’s good days
and bad days ; he knows when he has been
ill, how ill he has been, and how far it is
safe to contradict him. He gauges accurately
by his parent’s countenance how his dinner
is digesting, and if his liver is all right. He
knows the meaning of every twitch of the
Judge’s eyebrows, of every movement of his
body ; and drops or presses his argument
accordingly. Perhaps he still lives in his
father’s house ; anyhow, he has lived with
him till manhood, has most likely been his
associate, and knows his views exactly upon
(at least some) doubtful points of law. What
a tremendous handicap does this son take
into the race !
» * •
A third way out of the difficulty would be
to invite the Judges not to make lawyers of
their sons ; but this way also is hopeless,
for the answer to the invitation is generally
“No” in advance (though one highly-placed
Australian Judge has set a good example
by making his heir an engineer. As a
rule, every Judge makes lawyers of his
sons—just as his father the Judge made a
lawyer of him—from sheer force of habit,
and because he cannot conceive a son of his
being anything but a lawyer. Thus it is
that in some Australian provinces the legal
descendants of certain old-time Judges are
almost too numerous to count; while other
Judges have one, two, or three sons or other
relatives practising before them at the Bar.
In one province, an ex-Judge’s four or five
sons are all either solicitors or barristers,
while other Judges have sons growing
up to practise under papa’s wing in the
usual course. In several provinces the
Judge’s associate is just as inevitably his
son whenever it can be managed. So
that to expect the ruck of Judges to
make engineers, or doctors, or publicans
of cheir sons is to expect the impossible.
There remains only one thing to be done :
let the Judges of every Australian province
petition Parliament to pass a short Act to
prevent their near relatives by blood or
marriage from practising before them. This
would at once remove suspicion from the
minds of the public, establish a better feel-
ing among members of the legal profession,
spare the" Judges many annoying miscon-
structions, and markedly facilitate the ad-
ministration of justice.
Federation.
Give me at once good, wholesome-hearted
federation :
I believe in it, work for it, long for it—
A nation of true people proud and complete in
their own throbbing isle.
Not a dead image, a cold, empty sepulchre,
A mere compend of current hypocrisies and
conventionalities,
To be founded by folly, legalised by force.
Not a storehouse of newspaper fads, and string-
pulling politicians’ puerilities:
Something with heart and blood and brain at
the back of it.
Warm with the whole-souled concurrence of
the people ;
Void of the jealousies of cities and Parliaments,
Of the petty quibbles of rival functionaries,
Plot and intrigue, and sordid calculation.
Something a little more than ceremonial,
Above mean manoeuvres, narrowness, shallow-
ness,
The parochialism of miserly land-owners.
A tower to bear all the blasts of the centuries,
A voice to trumpet truth over every land and
sea of this shining globe,
A torch eternally beaconing humanity onward.
If not this, then not anything ;
I will have no compromise, no subterfuge, no
sweet opiate,
Better carelessness, silence, uncomprehending
apathy.
J.C.M.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Not an Imperial Fad Oration.
Col. Bull, U.S.A. Consul-General of Australia,
addressed the Imperial Federation League in
Melb. Town Hall, the other day, with very
little satisfaction to the League, if the de-
meanour of the platform figureheads may be
taken as a guide. From start to finish the
Colonel preached Socialism, and the blank
dismay on the platform was a joyful sight.
Melbourne has a host of people of the Zox and
Downes Carter type who pose publicly at
every opportunity. Lately they have had
bad times. At the Australian Federation
meeting, Kingston preached one-man-one-vote.
At the big bi-metallic meeting, Morton
Frewen preached Protection, and before this
Jingo meeting Colonel Bell preached Socialism.
The Colonel likewise preached Freetrade. But
it was a Freetrade so ameliorated by Socialism
as to be utterly abhorrent to the Cobber
school. The Colonel contrasted the magnificence
of millionaires with the poverty of the people
—the increasing wealth of monopolies with the
little advantage accruing to the mass of the
community from invention and industrial pro-
gress. For 100 minutes the eloquence lasted,
with a wealth of rhetoric and gesticulation. The
result was paradoxical. The Tories heard an
advocacy of Freetrade that horrified them. The
Radicals found themselves admiring an impos-
sible combination of Freetrade and Socialism.
When the orator launched into extravagant
praises of England’s might, the jingoes enthused
tremendously, and when he declared that it was
all as nothing if the United States did not back
it up, they enthused still more. That bellige-
rent, Freetrade England, with her forts in
every sea, should be nothing without the Pro-
tectionist, unarmed States, was a hard pill to
swallow —yet down it went. But the loudest
cheer of all came full-throated from every
man in the hall when Col. Bell demanded
that Australia should take her place among the
nations. It would be hard to call an ideal, so
eloquently outlined, a bagman’s millennium.
To make trade the end and all of man’s life is
the Freetrader’s aim, but not Col. Bell’s. He
proposes to have Freetrade with the competi-
tive system eliminated, so that the vested
rights of labor shall get recognition, and paten-
tees and land-monopolists shall no longer ex-
ploit the earnings of the community. This is
not Freetrade—lt is an impossible form of Pro-
tection. The Bulletin will stick to its own
practicable policy.
Knapp, the Governor-Maker.
Blue Ribbon Knapp, the persistent Sydney
letter-writing bore, who, as a publicist, is an
equal mixture of the fierce Italian greyhound
and the carnivorous pink-eyed rabbit, suggests
that Chief Justice Barley be appointed (Gover-
nor of New South Wales. Poor Chief Justice
Darlby ! Knominated by Knapp ! In advo-
cating the appointment, Knapp eloquently
remarks that it would “seal the tender tie, the
crimson thread, which binds Australia to the
mother land.” Of course, the bathetic Knapp’s
suggestion is mischievous balderdash and
mere self-advertising froth, since there is
not the least probability of the Crown taking
the hint. Apart from that, the idea, as
John Ballance pointed out, is bad demo-
cratic diplomacy. While the Crown retains
the right of nomination, it is belter that
foreigners should be nominated for Australian
Governorships, and the foreign character of the
office kept thus well in view of the people. We
only want local men as Crown representatives
when the choice rests entirely with our-
selves—that is to say, never ; since when
the Australian provinces elect their own presi-
dents or vice-presidents, the Crown will be
represented no longer. All that is worth con-
tending for, until the present system gives way
to a better, is the right of Australian veto upon
the appointment of too objectionable foreigners.
It is a moot point, to be sure,-whether it would
not be desirable to get as Governor the most
objectionable foreigner possible —even a para-
sitic Battenberg —in order that popular disgust
with the foreign connection might become still
more thorough. But while bheCrown nominates,
unless the right of veto is definitely conceded to
Parliament, the local man is the last man wanted.
For whom would the Crown choose? Simply
the Fattest, the most undemocratic, the most
truckling, grovelling personage it could find.
The man of all others whom the people disliked
most cordially, the man most loyal to the
Crown, most disloyal to Australia, would un-
doubtedly be nominated. And then, when
Democracy turned against him, it wmuld be met
with the reply : “ But he is a local man ; he is
one of yourselves !” and his office would gain
under this cloak of locality a representative
character which it can never have at present.
Elect our own Governor —good ! Accept an
Australian citizen as Governor at England s
mere nomination —No !

The Boodleland Squatters.
Dear Bulletin, —Re par. of Feb. 16 on decline
of West Queensland towns. The cause is simply
the manner in which the country is governed for
the benefit of the big squatter, while the small
grazier is not allowed a footing at any price.
Take Aramac for example. We have some
of the finest grazing country in Australia, and
can also obtain artesian water in any quantity
at from 700 to 1400 ft. The district is possibly
the most suitable in Queensland for division
into grazing selections, each not less than 5000
acres; but it is at present occupied by half a
dozen large stations —this fact alone, with its
attendant curse of absenteeism, being the cause
cf the stagnation round Aramac. The only graz-
ing selections thrown open here have been
rushed, and are paying handsomely ; and if
there were 100 thrown open to-morrow, 20,000
acres in extent, there are people in the im-
mediate neighborhood eager to take them up,
without speaking of the dozens continually
coming from the South. Recently Govt, threw
open a number of selections some 150 miles away
—not comparable in value with wdiat you could
get round here. Enquiries for them came even
from South Australia, Victoria, and New South
Wales ; yet at the same time Ministers profess
that the country is in great straits financially,
though they are accepting a fraction of Id. rent
per sq. mile where they might be receiving U
or 2d. per acre. For some inscrutable reason
the selections referred to have now been with-
drawn—why I can’t say, but it was certainly
not for want of selectors for those areas large
enough to keep a man and his family. Some
of the areas thrown open were so small that they


were useless for settlement. As you know, there
are other ways ofkilling a cat than choking it
with cream. What Aramac wants is selection,
and this, unluckily, is what Govt, does its best
to prevent. A police reserve of 1000. acres was
made here, hounded on one side by the Aramac
reserve and on the opposite side by a small
selection of 1280 acres. Sometime ago the
police reserve was found to he unsuitable, and,
upon application being made, was thrown open
at a rental of 2s. per acre ! The adjacent selec-
tion, similar in every respect except size, is
rented at 3d. per acre, and the occupant is
barely able to support his family. The absur-
dity of the 2s. rent has been pointed out, hut
without result. This will give you some idea
why Western Queensland doesn’t go ahead. Pro-
gress is throttled by the vast squattages, with
Govt, conniving.—Yours, Equity, Aramac.
The Coming" Figurehead.
Pending alteration of the provincial constitu-
tions, Australian Governors must be paid ; hut
there is no necessity to pay them extrava-
gantly. Iti the past the extravagance has been
beyond question. Some Governors have drawn
in cash and perquisites not less than £15,000 a
year—an amount quite out of proportion to the
merit of the recipient, to the value of the ser-
vice, to the character of the office, to the popu-
lation and resources of the province which paid
it. When the President of the United States
is adequately paid at £lO,OOO a year, the
Governor of an Australian province may very
well do with £5OOO. That is the amount
received by the Governors of Queensland, of
South Australia, and of Maoriland ; and it is
quite enough for the Governors of New South
Wales and Victoria. Victoria has lately with-
drawn from the insane rivalry with the older
province, and the imminent Brassey is
to get £7OOO a year with house—and
no pickings. The figure would probably
have been £5OOO, as fixed by the Assembly,
hut for complications alleged to have arisen
through the previous offer of the larger sum.
New South Wales can easily avoid complica-
tions by fixing at once at £5OOO the salary
to be. paid to Gov. Duff’s successor. No
man will earn more : it is all but a certainty
that the man who comes will earn very much
less. The dignity of the office need not be
taken into consideration. If the Imperial
authorities wish their nominee to maintain the
dignity which naturally hedges the representa-
tive of a Royal family averaging sft. high arid
3ft. thick or thereabouts, the Imperial authori-
ties should find the cash. The people of N.S.
Wales are not concerned with Royalty’s alleged
dignity or absence of dignity, and the new
Governor can keep his representative capacity
in the coal-scuttle for all the Australian people
cares. Nor is it desired or desirable that a
Governor should he furnished with a cash
surplus for the entertainment of “ society ”
toadies. Anyway, the men and women who
crawl round the doors of Government House
will not be discouraged by the absence of
food and drink, should a retrenched viee-gerent
find it necessary to stop the issue of sand-,
wiches and bad whisky taken free out of
bond. Merely to bask in the reflected light of
Her G racious is all that they require. They
erawl by instinct—not for their suppers. And
New South Wales, and every other pro-
vince in Australia, can find much better
uses for the people’s money than to devote
it to the swilling and" guzzling of metro-
politan silvertaiK especially when thou-
sands iu the metropolis and thousands “ out-
back ” do not know to-day where to-mor-
row.’s tucker is coming from. The reduction
might very well go beyond £5009, ..but £5OOO
will do for the present. It goes one better than
Victoria, which may stimulate province-
to cut Brassey’s successor down to £4OOO,
in which case there is nothing to prevent New
South Wales offering £3OOO. That is a sane
kind of rivalry which The Bulletin would
be glad to see. But, in the present, for New
South Wales, £5OO0 —and no perks. Let the
Labor-party strain their souls for that. The
house and the £5OOO, but not one penny beyond,
on whatever ingenious pretext demanded. Perks
are the ruin of straight finance, the abhorrence
of every properly-eonstituted democrat. Better
£lO,OOO right out than £5OOO and ever-expand-
ing perks. The Governor’s man-servant and
his maid-servant, his secretaries and officials,
his carriages and horses and forage—let him
pay for all out of his own pocket, and console
himself, as a sensible Governor will, with the
thought that a Governor’s liquors and cigars
are duty-free (for which there is no good reason
whatever). With whisky at half-a-crown a
.gallon and tobacco at a shilling a pound, the
Governor who can’t console himself isn’t worth
consoling.
The Passing of Hopetoun.
Lord Hopetoun is the Melbourne topic of the
week. Lord Hopetoun, after being banqueted
and farewelled and officially worshipped in a
lavish manner, has quitted the scene of his vice-
regal labors (unless something went wrong with
his'travelling arrangements) to the accompani-
ment of cannons’ roar. The local army lined the
streets, the local Fat Man waved Ids influential
head-gear, and the local populace did not spoil
their Governor’s exit by laughing at its exagge-
rated importance. Among the large and grow-
ing majority of thinking persons who object to
any sort of monarchial representation for social
reasons, and who honestly believe that a
Governor imported solely on account of his
birthright position is an undesirable resi-
dent—among the anti-loyalists, in short
Lord Hopetoun was ' kindly thought of,
when thought about at all. He walked
through the colorless character of Gover-
nor with a springy step and a propitiatory
smile. Appearing younger than his age, the
slim earl prudently resolved to be even younger
than his appearance, and pleaded to the com-
munity, by word and manner, for special con-
sideration on account of his inexperience, if;
presented himself as a light-hearted n >\ .oc
sojourning in Victoria for the benefit of his
In-alth, and feeling grateful to the climate that
suited his delicate constitution. Hence Lord
Hopetoun’s negative popularity with tax-
payers in general. He was a refreshing change
from the old vice-regal stamp of person who
assumes that his exalted presence is laying the
country under an obligation to him. Australians,
all over the continent, take credit to themselves
for Australian sunshine. We speak of the
weather with patriotic pride. Consequently,
Gov. Hopetoun’s frequent acknowledgments of
favors received from the atmosphere commended
the weak-chested peer to his fellow breathers,
and they were sorry to witness the departure of
one who regretted having to go ’Ome to study
his rent-roll. The spectacle was almost pathetic.
A pallid “-boy-Governor ” tearing himself away
from the sanatorium where he had been feeling
quite strong and well, bai jove ! for the past
few years. Sickly globe-trotters come and
go without exciting the least public in-
terest, hut Gov. Hopetoun was the clean-
shaven Gov. who cost them more than
£20,000 a year to entertain, and they cheered
him accordingly. A less objectionable Governor
Victoria had never known. Famous for nothing
save a fainting fit at his first levee, he left with
flying colors. When we come to remember
that Lord Hopetoun carried a handsome but
unsociable wife on his shoulders, so to speak,
and yet managed to get through the vice-regal
job without a hitch, we are struck by the idea
that a lame young Governor, embellished with
a fine, hacking cough and a respirator, would
tighten the silken bonds considerably.
The reciprocity-treaty which is being nego-
tiated between South Australia and Maoriland
promises to be an awkward stumbling-block in
the road of Federation. In its present form
the agreement provides for the free admission
of S.A. wines and a few other articles into
Maoriland in return for the free admission into
S.A. of Maoriland oats, &c. This arrangement
is to endure for seven years, and while it lasts
each contracting party is absolutely prohibited
from entering into any commercial treaty what-
ever with any other province of Australasia.
If Maoriland joins the Federation—supposing it
to come along within the seven years—the
treaty makes no difference, for Federation will
abolish all interprovincial tariffs and allow for
free interchange of everything within the limits
of the Commonwealth. But Maoriland has
definitely announced her intention of standing
out and playing a lone hand. Therefore, when
the Federal Parliament undertakes to establish
a uniform Australian tariff against the outside
world it will be compelled to make an excep-
tion in favor of Adelaide so far as the
Maoriland goods covered by the treaty are
concerned. And as there will be no border
Customs-houses, the Maoriland goods which
came in free at Adelaide can also get access to
the other provinces, so that there will be a large
hole in the Federal tariff. It is not likely that
the trade between the two provinces will
amount to much, but the negotiation of a treaty
with such provisoes means that, for seven years
to come, S.A. will make the establishment of a
simple and uniform Australian tariff impossible,
and will do it for the smallest imaginable in-
ducement. The willingness of the Kingston
Government,' for such a very questionable
make Federation a little more diffi-
cult tllan it already is, doesn’t augur well for
the future. A few more treaties of the same
kind'would make Federation impossible for
years to come.

~Dear Bulletin,— That contest between Man-
chester cotton and India is by no means new.
Here is an extract from Carey : “ Cotton
abounded, and half-a-century since (1807)
, native labor not only supplied the Home
demand but produced a hundred millions of
pounds of cloth per annum for export to dis-
tant countries. Exchange was so much in
favor of India that a rupee now (1857) worth
44 cents was then worth no less than 64 cents.
The company had a monopoly of collecting
taxes, but, in return, it preserved to the people
the control of their domestic market. Such
protection was required, because while Eng-
land prohibited the export of a single collier
who might instruct the people in mining coal,
of a steam-engine or the mechanic who might
make one, of a powerloom, or of the artisan
who could give to the poor Hindoo instruction
iu the use of such machines, she, at the same
time, imposed heavy duties on the produce of
Indian looms received in Britain. Later, how-
ever, the trade to India was thrown open, the
restrictions on the export of machinery and
artisans being still maintained in full force, and
thus a poor and ignorant people were exposed
to competition with a community possessed of
far more effective implements. Twenty years
later, we find a whole year to have passed
without the export of a single piece of cloth.”
-Y.
Melbourne Metropolitan Board of Works has
at last carried an abstract resolution in favor of
the minimum wage. Discussing the matter, the
Argus describes the speech of Commissioner
Talbot as “the most notable made on the
question.” This brilliant person’s oration is
summarised as follows :
There are a large number of laboring men in Brunswick
who are fathers of families and ratepayers and have been
out of work for 12 months. These men, who are middle-
aged, would he glad to get work at small wages, hut if a
minimum rate is fixed above the ruling market rate they
will get none at all.
As the minimum wage is 65., it would be in-
teresting to know what Talbot and the Argus
quote as “ the ruling market rate ” for “ labor-
ing men in Brunswick who are fathers of
families.” It is about time the Argus began to
give labor-market quotations in its commercial
column something after this style ;
Labor. —The local market in labor is steady at from
2s. 6d. to 3s. 5d., supplies offering liberally. A fair busi-
ness was done at 2s. 9d., and one lot of pick-and-shoyel
hands, slightly damaged, went at 2s. 3|d. Inferior
laborers have been quoted as low as 2s. 2d. For prime
samples, young and sound, 3s. was offered, and taken in
several places. The medium and off qualities were avail-
able from Is. 9d. and upwards. The market promises to
be easy through the week and buyers are cautious.
According to the decision of the Vic. Cabinet
last week in the cases of two individuals under
“sentence of death recorded” for the commis-
sion of offences of imbecile obscenity, JO lashes
are equivalent to five years’ imprisonment. One
miserable wretch, who had been unfortunate
enough to “ pass the doctor,” was sen-
tenced to 10 years and two floggings
t ■
of 15 lashes each : the other, the
Cabinet had beep “informed,” was of “weak
intellect,” and got 15 years. By the natural
operation of these penalties the alleged healthy-
minded committor of an unclean act will —
supposing he is, as implied, a brute to begin
with—come out of prison a worse and more
degraded brute than ever ; while the imbecile
will become more pronouncedly weak-minded
and fit for the lunatic asylum, to which he
should have been committed iu the first in-
stance. The sentences imposed by the Judges
are often illogical enough, and it would appear
that sentence by Cabinet is just as had.
Land-alienation brings other curses in its
train. In New South Wales there is a demand
for postponement of selectors’ arrears till the
next generation. In Victoria, also, the selectors
have fallen into arrears with their payments, and
an agonised wail goes up from among them when
the Treasury demands its dues. The mort-
gagees join in the wail, and the Tory press,
and all the noble army of croakers—and a nice
time the Minister of Lands has with them all.
The Minister asks for money and the selectors
ask for time—as time and money are both
unemployed in Victoria now they ought
to get it, but they don’t. The evil is
accentuated by the immoral Australian custom
of paying proceeds of land-sales into revenue,
instead of into a sinking-fund. But the root of
the evil is the land-alienation. The selectors’
payments should be pure rent, not alleged pro-
gress-payments on an alleged capital value. The
selectors should be tenants pure and simple,
and their rent should be fixed from year to
year iu accordance with the actual value of the
property they rent. As for the mortgagees
who have advanced moneys at excessive rates
on the poor security a selector in arrears could
give—they can stand the loss which their rates
of interest proves them to have expected.
Bowen (Q.) State-railway is one of the natural
curiosities of Australian political railway sys-
tems. It meanders into the vast interior for 50
miles, and abruptly deposits you in the middle
of a forest. Nobody lives in the forest, or has
ever lived there, and uobody ever wants to go
there, and no goods can he taken there, because
there is no one to take them to ; so two trains
run out to the forest every week with the
engine-driver and fireman and guard and station-
master, just to see if the forest is growing in
the same place, and when it is found all right
the trains just come hack again. This enables
the Bowen people to talk of “our railway,”and
gives the local editor (who is about the weariest
man in Australia, and very sick with hope
deferred) a chance to dilate upon the “ magni-
ficent future ” in store for Bowen when the line
is extended to the Reid river, 40 miles from the
present forest terminus. The line was origin-
ally meant to be extended to the Reid river,
but jealous Townsville influence managed to
block the extension at the outset, and it has
merely been “going to be extended” ever since.
The inhabitants who witnessed the turning of
the first sod are getting gray anti toothless now,
hut they die off cheerfully in the hope of a
blessed resurrection in time to see “the exten-
sion.”
The people of Melbourne are considering the
advisability of establishing a free port —Wil-
liams to wu will probably he the place selected—
and if this valuable idea is not smothered by
the hideous slowness and inefficiency of Parlia-
mentary procedure it will probably be carried
out —some day. Even a simple and obviously
useful idea, however, to which uobody has any
serious objection, is so apt to come to grief on
the shoreless sea of Legislative formality that
no one can even guess when the proposed
event is likely to happen. The scheme itself is
one which can he explained in a few words.
Melbourne does a large distributing trade,
goods being landed there from over-sea, to he
re-shipped to other provinces. The State col-
lects duty on these goods, which involves a
good deal of trouble and stationery, and a lot of
money temporarily out of pocket; and when they
are re-shipped it bauds back the duty as “draw-
back.” This involves more time and trouble and
stationery. Also, the processes cost some small
amount in fees. The importer loses the
fees and the time and the interest on the money
paid as duty, and the State loses time also, and
wear and tear of officials and stationery, and
gains nothing whatsoever. The establishment
of a free port is intended to obviate all this.
At such a port all goods will be landed at the
lowest possible wharfage and other charges, and
they will be stored without paying any duty or
going through any needless formalities. If
they leave the store to be used or sold iu Vic-
toria, then duty will be charged ; if they leave
it to be re-shipped they will go out free as they
came in. If Victoria establishes a free port,
and the other provinces don’t follow suit, then
Melbourne will become the distributing centre
for Australia, and will annex whatever profit
there may he in the trade. The idea is so
simple, and the saving of time and worry and
stationery and money ,so obvious, that it is
strange no one ever thought of it before, and
still stranger, now that somebody has thought
of it, that it will probably take five or six
years for anything to be done in the matter.
According to St. James’s Gazette, much dis-
satisfaction is expressed in “ the higher walks
of theatrical life ” at the rule which prevents
actors and actresses being received at Court. It
doesn’t say much for the souls of mummers in
higher walks. No true artist ever hankered
after the cheap and empty social distinction
conferred by newspaper reports. The glory of
a presentation at Court begins and ends in
press mention of that solemnly ridiculous cir-
cumstance. The Queen receives only “ gentle-
folks.” She limits her circle of formal acquaint-
ance, even as an ordinary vulgar snob is careful
not to become friendly with persons of seedy
appearance. If you manufacture beer or had
spirits for the destruction of the human race
you may get yourself into the royal outer circle
and your name into the Court Journal. As
for the rule relating to these grovelling actors, it
applies to about 99 19-20 per cent, of Her
Majesty’s loyal subjects in Great Britain. The
crowd of unrecognised toilers who cannot enter
Court includes great literary men who write in
a democratic spirit. The sjtob’s Heaven is not
for such as Charles Dickens, or William
Makepeace Thackeray, although a wealthy
publisher may manage to get -a ticket of admis-
sion if he cares to lose money by bringing out a
three-volume novel fora countess.
The London Times says : “ Speaking at a
meeting of the Council of the Liverpool Cham-
ber of Commerce, last week, Mr. Bindloss
made rather startling reference to the diversion
of British iron and steel trades to other
countries. Liverpool, he said, had benefited
largely from exports to the U.S. in the past,
but of late years the States had developed their
own resources, and by heavy tariffs had so
stimulated production that they could not only
supply themselves, but severely interfered
with England in foreign markets, and be
would not be surprised if they by-and by
threatened competition even in this country. A
Pennsylvania company had lately received from
the Russian Government an order for 12,000
tons of armor-plates, work hitherto given to
Sheffield. This meant a difference of £500,000
or £600,000 to that town. Our trade with Italy
was also being threatened, and, last of all,
Canada, which had cost more in blood and
treasure than all the other colonies, had imposed
a most severe tariff, which practically meant
the annihilation of our trade with that country.
We had also to face an intense German com-
petition with India, and, indeed, with all parts
of the world.”
Dear Bulletin,—A cricket cablegram : “ The
Times says the Australians accepted their
defeat with true English generosity.” Can
anybody supply a belter example of smug, self-
satisfied egotism ? Who hut the insular Briton
would make himself a standard of measure-
ment for the virtues of the rest of the
world ? Does “ English generosity ” differ
from the article in use among the lower races
of mankind? From French generosity, say? or
from generosity made in Germany ? Hospitality
is the better half of generosity. In Australia
and Maoriland the British globe-trotter meets
with a hospitality that surprises him. The
surprise is significant. Up-country stations
welcome him, a perfect stranger, with a
lavish warmth which he himself would
hardly bestow on a life-long friend. The
household sacrifices everything to provide him
amusement; riding, hunting, shooting —he
has only to breathe his whim. What
Australasian hospitality may be like is shown
by the adventure of an English lady who
told the writer that, while travelling on one of
the rivers of Northern Maoriland, she called in
at a settler’s homestead" for a glass of water;
and, though an entire stranger, remained a
guest in the family for nsix weeks. Imagine
such a scene occurring in England. The warm-
blooded “colonial” stepping as a matter of
course from his punt; the cold, superior stare of
the footman as he directs him to the servants’
entrance ; or the red and wrathful rudeness of
the proprietor of the mansion instructing him
in the law of trespass, and commending him to
the waters of the river ! “ English generosity ”
is a figment, a tradition ! It has taken flight
beyond seas, along with many other stalwart
virtues which have found themselves cramped
by the commercial slavery of the last half-
century. The Englishman of to-day lives on
the credit accumulated by his ancestors : he is
swollen by conceit beyond all tolerance. Some-
body will stick a bayonet into him one of these
days, and the gas will escape in a liurrv
Yours, S.C.W.

At Strathalhyn (S.A.) the monotony of the
last monthly sale was changed to excitement when
a very prominent old resident was seen, whip in
hand, chasing his son (a 40-year-old) around the
stockyards, the “young ’un” having failed to
comply with a parental demand. Unable to catch
his nimble offspring, the grey-haired dad went for
the son’s buggy, smashing up the wheels with an
axe. This did not suffice to cool the old man’s
anger, and sonny’s house was next visited, the
furniture having to suffer.
Five years ago, or thereabouts, a settler
near Wellington (M.L.) lost a horse. He reported
the fact to the police, and the animal was put on
the list of stolen property, and ever since then the,
authorities have looked for it at intervals, and
never came any nearer to finding it—until the
other day, when the animal suddenly dawned on
tiiem, in a manner of speaking. The thief had
doctored the brands a little, banged the animal’s
tail and out his mane, and had sold him to the
police department. Since then he has done many
a long day’s run while out looking for himself, and
has come back again without seeing any sign of
himself on the furthest horizon. How that horse
must have laughed !
Dear Bulletin, —lf N.S. W. Labor Bureau
doesn’t bang Banagher, then Banagher has never
been bung. There was no scarcity of labor
up country when—or since—the Bureau com-
menced operations. The labor sent from the
metropolis on passes has had the effect of lower
ing wages and supplanting employes. Most hush
workers out of employment now would have a
better chance of getting work through the Bureau
than by remaining in their habitat. The em-
ployer has a large faith in the chastening influence
of the city unemployed mill, and expects from the
imported article a cheap subservience he cannot
get from the man who was never minus mutton
to his damper. What a hubbub would there be
amongst the organised laborers of Sydney if a
few hundreds of unemployed hushmen got an
outfit and free passes to Sydney for the ostensible
purpose of fossicking for fish in “our beautiful
harbor ! ” Yet such a proceeding would be just
as sensible as sending “all sorts and conditions of
men ” to fossick for gold where Chinamen won’t
put a pick in. A return showing how many of
the “fossickers” are still fossicking, and how-
many have entered into the bush labor competi-
tion would be instructive. But the end is not
yet! The Labor Bureau as at present conducted
means the centralisation of the labor-market, with
the eventual result of making Sydney the depfit
for the unemployed of the whole province.—
Treble B.

My Dear Moorabinda—
The vice-regal demise has given a vast number
of aggressive nobodies a golden chance to get
their names into the papers which fatuously
chronicle such very small-beer, and apparently
don't know the sheep from the goats. Strange
people have been calling at Govt. House in
awkward squads, and writing their names down
in the vice-regal book, for all the world as if
the bereaved chatelaine intended giving a fare-
well party and they wanted to be in it.
Some of the visitors who forced their sympathy
on Lady Duff in the early stages of her bereave-
ment were highly indignant at not being allowed
an actual interview with the mourner. They
yearned to be a sister to her in the saddest
hour of her life, and wanted to hold her hand
and lend her their clean pocket-handkerchief —
also, they felt a strong desire to know how the
vice-regal mourning was cut. So, when they
wei ! politely blocked on the mat and told to
couhde their emotions to a blank page, they
: 1 most refused to do so. One pushing and well-
: dvertised lady did bounce off the premises.
There are two visitors’-books at Govmentouse
at present—one for the Lieut.-Governor and one for
Lady Duff, and I am sure it must be edifying
to see the tearful more or less society “lydies”
inscribing their sympathy in the latter, and then,
brightening up at the thought of possible junket-
ings, dodge over to set down their names
in the former with a view to being armed at all
points. In the midst of death we are very much
alive. Sorrow with the widow and the fatherless;
but don’t let your grief blind you to the fact that
there may be an acting-vice-regal garden-party or
two when the month of State mourning has passed.
I have been a good deal amused, by the way, at
the said State mourning. Attempts at it have occa-
sionally verged on the grotesque, particularly in
the case of a prominent lady in a loud dress with
a clumsy cloth badge and a sort of cook’s cap of
crape. Another damsel’s “ State mourning ” took
the form of a white dress, a green sash, and black
crape rosettes in the front of her gown and hat;
and a third, in a lizard-colored frock, was evi-
dently enjoying the effect of her deep badge and
crape flowers, for she was smiling from ear to ear.
These models were seen haphazard in the streets,
of course ; one section of society was much more
thorough in the inky-cloak business.
All who know anything of the black and bitter
past of N.S. Wales, including especially the days
oftheKiama Ghost, must deeply regret the so-
far-uncontradicted statement that a lady in a high
civic position has absolutely refused to patronise
a certain school festival promoted by people of a
different creed, and has set it down in black and
white that she cannot possibly have anything to
do with R.C, functions, X take it that in these com-
paratively happy and tolerant days official people
should have no religion, but should cheerfully
patronise a Moslem feast and be ready to finish
the night at a Presbyterian prayer-meeting. Un-
less the lady effectively denies the sentiment im-
puted to her there will be much trouble. No one
known in advance to be dominated by the senti-
ments attributed to her could ever be pub-
licly chosen for the position she now holds. There
can be no doubt as to the proper social status of any-
body who, in “ this alleged nineteenth century,”
quarrels about religion with a decently-behaved
neighbor.
I see that Acting-Governor Darley has arranged
his staff wherewith to run the province until some
confiding English peer whose estates need nursing
comes forth to release him from social bondage.
The popular Mr. Bayley Macarthur is to be pri-
vate secretary, vice Mr. Harry Darley—now, I
believe, at Coolgardie where the typhoid-stories
come from-—while the lemon-tinted Captain Feild-
ing takes the place of the plump and pleasing
Captain Horace Dangar, who is now cramming
military knowledge into himself with a ramrod at
’Ome.
I have received from the adaptor, Mr. F. R.
Peel, a rhythmic arrangement, in waltz-time, of
the übiquitous “ Sweet Marie ” song made popular
by Mr. Norrie. Mr. Peel has dished up a very
swinging waltz, easy to play and pretty all
through, and people who play “extras” should
add it to their repertoires at onoe.
From Mr. John L. Hordern I have received a
catalogue of the latest and extremest fashions.
My soul shrinks before the awful news that a pair
of sleeves will out into six yards of stuff and that
skirts must be five-and-a-half yards round still.
But it is the kindest thing, I suppose, to let
women know the worst at once.
If the new fashions are set by society leaders
abroad, all I can think is that they must be a very
defective and skinny lot. Everything is baggy
and saggy to conceal our faults rather than to
reveal imperious charms. These are lovely times
for a girl with one shoulder higher than the other,
big feet, and narrow hips, and awful periods for
“ a fine figure of a woman ” who frantically braves
criticism sometimes in a tight and becoming
bodice and gets stared at by her own sex as if she
was a waxworks. One reassuring circumstance
about the autumnal and winter fashion-books the
shops are sending out just now is that there isn’t
even a hint of the threatened knickerbocker suit,
and no diagrams are given encouraging women
down the cloven way that leads to bifurcation.
Apropos of the bifurcated, I am told that in
Maoriland, where knickers have indisputably
caught on, you may witness the incongruous spec-
tacle of women in trouserettes wearing net veils
over their faces ! Woman’s innate and most de-
sirable vanity will always prevent her being out-
wardly quite like a man, thank goodness. She
will always have her vests shaped to her figure,
and tie her “coats” in at the back in order to
preserve the wavy outline. I really don’t think
she will ever get to look much more virile than a
pantomime “boy,” even if she does emphatically
break into breeches.
Certain fashionable circles in Maoriland are
torn with emotion over the rumors that the bride
of a few months ago has had her love’s young
dream turned into a nightmare. The bridegroom,
a good-looking youth, was reputedly wealthy, and
was prepared to do the thing well; the bride’s
pa, now reputedly un wealthy, threw no obstacles
in the way of a convincing ceremony, and the
marriage took place with circus-like glitter.
After the bow was tied (the marriage ceremony
of to-day is no knot) the happy pair left to take
the edge off the honeymoon. But in one of the
cities a mysterious female, with a solemn family
of three in tow, suddenly turned up and claimed
the happy bridegroom as her very own, the best of
the wedding-gifts were hypothecated, and hubby is
now afar off leaving both ladies behind him.
But men don’t always get the best of it like
that. For instance, it happened (in Fiji), some
years ago, that a pretty girl fell in love with a
society youth who promised her marriage “ when
his screw was raised,” but who ultimately deserted
her and married the only daughter of a wealthy
widower. Then the forlorn one took to hospital-
nursing in order to get the taste out of her
mouth, so to speak. After a lapse of years (every
thing comes to her who waits) the father-in-law
of her former flame became her patient, and she
nursed him so successfully and devotedly that the
widower, on his recovery, offered her his hand
and heart, both of which she gladly accepted.
Now, as mother-in-law to her false adorer, she
has got him under the whip. If I were that son-
in-law I would at once light out for the South
Pole.
Sydney Ladies’ Club is becoming mildly bohe-
mian in its middle age. From being the select
temple of the Sydney one hundred, and carrying
on behind hermetically-sealed doors, it now makes
a den of lions of itself by entertaining all the prin-
cipal actresses in town, and encouraging them
with tea and talk on special afternoons. Mrs.
Brough (whose notion of recreation is a long day’s
fishing) was solemnly entertained by the Club
with one or two of the younger actresses of the
co. ; Mrs. Bland Holt and Miss Spong have re-
ceived its tame hospitalities once or twice, and the
last Thespienne to be made free of the premises was
the forceful and imposing Geraldine Olliffe.
The public-ball season promises to rage fiercely
in May, and what I believe will be the first public
dance of any social weight will be the ball in aid
of the Women’s Hospital at Lewisham, to eventu-
ate in the Centennial Hall on Bth May. Tickets
at prices to meet the times, and many good names
on the patrons’ list. Mrs. (Dr.) Maguire and
Mrs, Wynn Roberts are hon. secs. The late
Governor Duff had given his patronage, and his
name still sadly adorns the tickets.
If the stage really holds the mirror up to
Nature, this must be the age of talkee-talkee. In
“Rebellious Susan,” the new advanced-woman
treatise at Sydney Lyceum, Titheradge, in beauti-
fully-fitting clothes, as the good genius of the
piece, only wanted a little table with a tumbler of
water on it and a fat man in the chair to have
been a series of highly-improving psychological
lectures —he had so many intricate orations to get
through. Everybody’s tongue seemed to be hung
in the middle so as to work easily both ways.
Fundamentally, “ Susan ” is not a knickerbocker
play—the dramatist evidently believes more
devoutly in woman’s sufferings than woman-suf-
frage, for of the two “new women” in the piece,
Susan looked silly and Elaine ridiculous. Mrs.
Quesnell (Miss Faber), who reminds me of Romeo
Greenaway, was the real heroine —an ideal “ old ”
woman, clever, softly-mannered, daintily-rigged,
and catching old birds with chaff as a natural con-
sequence. A refined and altogether charming
actress is Miss Faber, who, in the exquisite love-
scene with Titheradge, brought down the house as
i have not heard it brought down for years. By
the way, the “new” play seems co end up as
Noah’s Arkily as the old, for the couples all pair-off
for the final. Somebody ought to write a smart
play with no marriage in it, so as to give the grow-
ing hordes of he and she celibates a chance.
But, in spite of the all-saving prudence of the
Paragon female alluded to above, “ Susan” isn’t a
safe young lady for a married man to introduce
his wife to, as the carryings-on of the stage-charac-
ters sometimes leave a very lasting impression in
front. Take Watts-Phillips, for example. She is
going ’Ome shortly, whereat the married men
unanimously rejoice, for she was always uncon-
sciously teaching *heir wives to howl and squeal
and snivel and strike scornful and mock-heroic
stage attitudes in the sanctity of the domestic
hearth. So did Henrietta Watson, who set them
craning their necks like thirsty fowls and gurgling
in their conversation—so good riddance to both of
’em, say the married men. But I think they’ve a
still harder nut to crack in “Rebellious Susan”
Noble. Ravings and gurglings are really tributes
to the personal importance of Man, but a lady who
makes her triumphant exit snapping her fingers
under hubby’s astonished nose ere sallying forth
to pursue strange Johnnies through the social
wilderness is a far more risky variety to have
about the house.
The dressing is altogether lovely in “Susan.’
In the first act, Grace Noble sported a shot-pink-
and-blue silk, with folded bodice of blue velveteen
over a plain cloth vest. Miss Faber in cream,
with neck arrangement and long side-sash of brown
velvet, brown velvet toque ; and Emma Temple
in bright blue like a blue coot, with a velvet
bodice. In the second act, Miss Faber wore a
poetic confection in white chiffon, made with
many flounces edged with narrow salmon-colored
ribbon, with a frontal row of salmon-hued
rosettes—picture bodice and picture hat in
black, with a windmill-bow of salmon
chiffon A Grace Noble wore white silk, with
a purse-end sash of crepe—wide floral hat
en suite; and Emma Temple, in a reefer jacket
and a red vest, suggested yachting. In the last
act, Susan principally wears a handsome white
satin dinner-dress, with a tucker of silver and
scarlet, and braces of beaver fur covered at first
by a very scrumptious sacque of mottled pink and
white silk, with deep brown fur borderings, and
fastened at the throat with a paste buckle. Miss
Faber principally wears a black gown with jet
bodice and large red velvet sleeves—black and red
hat en suite —but Emma Temple, in a tweed suit
and a knock-about hat (in which she reminded a
score of people of a sort of glorified edition of
the woman who sells newspapers in King-street)
missed a splendid opportunity of testing the
public with the Bloomer costume in the last act.
There's a tip for the Divine Dot Boucicault.
Sydney society was astonished in the midst of
the social sackcloth-and-ashes of the Lenten
season and the mourning for the dead Governor
to read the curt announcement —even before the
engagement was whispered—of the marriage of
Mr. Allison, a Queensland squatter, with the
eldest daughter of Mr. and Mrs. Sydney Bur-
dekin. Owing to the circumstances aforemen-
tioned and to the sad bereavement lately suffered ’
by the bride’s' family, the ceremony was very
quiet, and the small wedding-party are said to
have looked like a needle in a bundle of hay—
having chosen the largest available church to get
married in. However, it’s not always the most
pompous ceremonials that hold the candle to the
happiest marriages.
People who were present,(l must say I wasn’t—
it was quite a men’s evening) at the preliminary
canter of Signor Lardelli’s new comic opera,
“ Katherine,” speak in hushed tones of deep rap-
ture about it. As Signor Lardelli intends
seriously producing it, legs and all, in the Big
Smoke where a good many shocking bad plays
come from, it is gratifying to know that
dilettanti like Mr. Bruce Smith, of the heavenly
moustache, call the composer of “ Katherine ”
the Australian Sullivan, and allude to his clever
librettist, Mrs. Marjory Brown, of Sydney, as a
“female Gilbert” who is responsible for a
“sparkling book.” lam told, however, that the
action of the opera is complete in the music and
that there is no spoken dialogue. This, if true,
is a pity, for a dialogue-less opera labors under a
heavy handicap.
Madame Gough, whose delightful translation of
Pailleron’s comedietta, : “ Le monde oh I’on
s’ennuie ” (the World of .'Boredom) kept the Rail-
way Institute full for two evenings, is to be con-
gratulated on a departure new in Australia, at
least. A clever translator of touch-and-go French
comedies, who wouldn’t use too much syrup of
Bowdler in the prescription, is just the person
Australia is howling for, it seems to me. For one
thing, Marcus Brutus Snap wouldn’t need to run
his “Australian rights” repertoire to rags and
tatters if there was a neat little lot of racy trans-
lations (not “ adaptations ”) to stop a hole to keep
the “frost” away.
Metropolitan Liedertafel concert in the big
Town Hall wasn’t a numerical success, owing to
the sudden attack of Wragge in the weather, but
the items went crisply. The 70 assisting chorus
ladies were by Florence Esdaile, Nora
O’Shaughnessy, and ’cello Strauss. Mr. J.
Edward Sykes and Mr. Ashcroft-Edwards deserve
congratulations on the spectacular and artistic
result. lam glad to see how finely Miss Esdaile
is coming along. Great things are predicted for
her—after she has gone through the inevitable Con-
tinental mill, of course—and her ultimate success
seems assured. Miss Esdaile’s triumphs will add
one more to the growing list of Victorian sopranos.
How is it that Australia’s sweetest warblers all
come from the cabbage-garden—and are nearly
all (like Miss Esdaile) of the Chosen ?
Yours affectionately, Sappho Smith.

« 1 —
“The Case of Rebellious Susan,” at
Sydney Lyceum, distinctly raises one’s estimate
of Henry Arthur Jones. It is an exceptionally
clever and amusing comedy, though it owes much
to the admirable interpretation of the Brough-
Boucicault company. Its success is due not to
intrinsic merit of the dialogue—which is never
witty in the fashion of, say, O oar Wilde—but to
the aptness with which the dialogue is fitted to
the situations, to the scope given for humorous
characterisation, and to the utilisation of the
hundred and one smart stage trickeries - chiefly
based on the contrast of incongruities—in which
Jones is such an adept. The plot is beautifully
managed. The interest begins when the curtain
rises, and never flags till it falls finally ; there is a
nice balancing of fun and sentiment; and some
half-serious, half-satirical treatment of the deep
and abiding sex-question removes any suspicion of
flippancy.
• • «
The play turns round the revolting
woman. Young Lady Susan Harabin’a husband
has been led away by unspecified Dejilahs, and his
sin has found him at home. Rebellious Susan
flouts the advice of her elders to submit to
woman’s destiny, scorns her husband’s easy con-
trition, and vows she will pay him back in his own
coin. When she has had her little romance, they
will meet on equal terms, and she will think about
forgiveness. So she travels with a lady friend to
Cairo, where there is a young man, and a memor-
able Sunday evening. Some discussion took place
in the English papers, on the original production
of the play, as to whether the romance went as far
as the fifth act; but Susan’s subsequent conduct,
and her lover’s stress on the fact that she can
trust him no more than she has done,
put doubt quite out of the question. Susan
returns, and there is even talk of a sixth act in
the shape of an elopement to Maoriland with the
lover; but Sir Richard Kato, Q.C., Susan’s
guardian, stops that. So the lover goes to Maori-
land alone, and faithlessly weds a charming
fellow-passenger. The distressed Susan then re-
turns to her distressed husband on a bygones-
bygones agreement, content to march with the
regiment now that she has vindicated her equal
right to break the ranks.
* # •
The most considerable.corner of the play
is borne up by Mp. Titheradge, who, as Sir
Richard Kato, pulls'all .the strings of the piece.
Ilia acting combines rare strength and restraint,
and leaves nothing to be desirstß Miss Noble, who
at times has barely shunned the gulf of pretty in-
sipidity, rises as rebellious Susan to the greatest
height she has attained, and played very finely in
the passionate scene at the close of Act 2. As Mrs.
Quesnel, a middle-aged chaperonish friend of
Susan’s, Mrs. Faber strengthened her Sydney
position considerably. There is something of
monotony in her voice and facial expression, but
she acts dexterously, and is particularly effective
in the jest-and-earnest love duet with Mr. Tithe-
radge. Mr. Boucicault’s perfection goes almost
without saying, and his representation of Fergus-
son Pybus, the genius who vainly seeks sym-
pathy and afflatus, was irresistibly ludicrous.
Miss Temple, another and most aggressive revolt-
ing woman, would heighten her effects by accen-
tuating the height and masculinity and general
ugliness of Elaine Shrimpton still further. Mr.
Carey (Admiral Darley) was good ; and Mr. Ward
(Lucien Edensor) and Miss Talbot (Lady Darley)
fair; but The Bulletin’s decision on the merits of
Mr. Dorringtqn is still postponed, for his compass-
legs and his air of asininity (the latter rather too
obvious towards the close) might either be his
general stock-in-trade or his not ineffective ad-
juncts to the r6le of Lady Susan’s husband. The
only noticeable deficiency lay with Mr. Compton,
who did not make Mr. Jacomb half so fussy and
conceited and pragmatical as he might be with
advantage.
** * *
“A Million of Money,” which throws a
glow of opulence over the last weeks of Bland
Holt’s panoramadramatio season at the Theatre
Royal, hasn’t visibly aged since the same gentle-
man produced it at the Lyceum nearly two years
ago. The red-headed divinity student is still red
headed, and probably wears the same wig; while
the siren who dies of hunger on a rock with an
insane husband bounding round her with a table-
knife, and the garish ass who squanders his
uncle’s money on the siren, and the saintly
wife who squanders her affection on the garish
ass, might all have been packed away with the
scenery for any evidence of wear and tear percept-
ible. However, the short-memoried public has
forgotten all about “ A Million of Money,” and a
fresh audience rolls up every night to chuckle
over the divinity student and groan at the villain
with all the original enthusiasm ; and the season
promises to end as it began—in a blaze of specta-
cular glory and a substantial plethora of cash.

Sydney Tivoli pursues its prosperous way
through a succession of new lulls, and new artists,
and new items, which are tak* n up and shed in
turn by an always well-dressed entertainment.
This week there are a new bas-o and anew tenor,
who are voted \ocal guns of the heaviest variety
calibre; and the elder brothers and sisteis of the
Tivoli are all as obvioqp and as amusing as ever.
Many London music-halls might envy Mr.
Rickards the company which he has now collected
after years of culling and training.

George Rignold opens Her Majesty’s again
on Saturday evening with an adaptation of
“Called Back” on a scale of immense magnifi-
cence—or, if there is anything vaster than immense
magnificence, then upon that scale. The Criterion
remains closed, though there are rumors of another
variety company contemplating entrance ; and the
Haunted Swing is turning people inside out and
suspending them over infinity by a single shoe-lace
for positively the last time or times.
** * *
Miss Amy Rosbile put in a reappearance
at the Melb. Bijou last Saturday, vice Miss
Harrie Ireland, whose glorious youtlifulness had
been her only failure in a middle-aged character.
Miss Roselle impersonates the virtuous matron of
the piece with all possible effect, but she is really
of less moment in “ A Bunch of Violets ” than
Miss Mabel Hardy. This lady’s discordant per-
formance was referred to last week. It is marked
by much misdirected earnestness and unac-
countable drapery, and the show is a
heavy sufferer thereby. Miss Muriel Carr
has taken over the ingenue part from an
amateur who didn’t make the most of her small
opportunities, and was not as satisfactory as the
new daughter, Violet, in the very important
matter of smita Miss Carr’s smile is not artless,
but it has an air of artlessless, also her figure is
fragile, and she drops her eyelids at the right
moment. Arthur Dacre, as the sentimental
bounder and chairman of “The Widow’s Mite,”
may be said to give a powerful representation of
himself. When his mannerisms are most stagey
they seem to be natural to Mr. D., and when hi
lines are cast in quiet places he seems the most
natural of actors. Mr. Daore’s performance im-
proves on acquaintance., Sydney Grundy’s play
is not calculated to grow upon the popular taste.
It jokes ponderously at the expense of labor-party
politics, and suggests, in a casual way, that work-
ing men are animated solely by beer. One only of
the male characters is distinguished for stainless
honor, and he is a noble lord. “A Bunch of
Violets ” may last over Saturday, and “ A Scrap
of Paper ” will follow.

** * *
The prevailing slimness of Melb. Royal
is said to be explained by a managerial oversight.
Irish drama is out of season during Lent, and
Charles Erin Verner has decided to withdraw
after Saturday. Also, he announces a firm resolve
not to appear again in Melbourne for a long, long
time. As Shaun the Post he will breathe his
last on the local stage, and meanwhile he takes a
benefit.
** * %
At the little Cambridge Theatre a good
first week’s business has rewarded the Vaudeville
Comedy Company for their pains. “ A Clerical
Error ” is sympathetically treated by Frank
Emery, Oathcart, Mario Majeroni, and Miss Ida
Gresham, although the two young people have yet
to pick up a lot of wrinkles. In “ The Amazon,”
which follows H. A. Jones’ comedy, three acts of
domestic uproar are perpetrated for all they are
worth by D’Arcy Stanfield, Miss Eva Clements,
Meta Pelham, Polly Emery, and a few other people
of a cheerful nature. There ought to be room in
Melbourne for this well-appointed show, at popular
prices. “The Snowball” rolls to the fore on
Saturday, probably. • Also, there are rumors con-
cerning the Cambridge and the sacred lamp of
burlesque, and already they have started sacred
concerts on Sunday nights.
Harry Rickards, armed with new songs
and provided with an entire change of programme,
is booming away at Melb. Opera House. The
galleries are always packed, and the ground floor
is invariably crowded, and the dress-circle is
gradually being jumped by a bloated aristocracy,
which has discovered that variety shows are quite
respectable—and only cost two shillings. The
Oxford stands where it did, whilst the Victor*
Hall opens and shuts up again at intervals.

The Court variety company opens in show-
desolate Brisbane on Ist April, at the Royal, and
if a little pains is taken there should be profit for
all concerned, so great is the public drouth.

The late George Lash Gordon came out
among the original B. and B. importations as a
sort of journeyman author, rather than actor. He
localised the first burlesques that B. and B. pro-
duced at Melbourne Opera House—those almost
forgotten shows in which Fanny Robina, Royce,
Brough, Florence Harcourt, Emma Temple and
Willie Warde were chief attractions, and Mrs.
Brough appeared as a dress-suited johnnie. Few
people seem to remember Paula Tanqueray, in
broadcloth pants, singing burlesque songs ; though
her as the Fairy Queen in “lolanthe” is
often referred to. Lash Gordon played a part in
B. and B.’s maiden production, the successful
“ Turned Up and died, as it happened, a mem-
ber of their company.

Another way of practically federating : Why
don’t the various Australian Govts, federate in placing
wines and produce 011 the European market? S.A. is
foremost as regards wine, and, by judicious advertising,
Agent-General Playford is making S.A. produce depot a
big success ; but, ere long, the different provinces will
start cutting prices and adopting all sorts of dodges to
secure the trade. Before matters go too far, the different
dep6ts should be amalgamated, and all Australian
wines, brandy, butter, and other produce sold, on com-
mission only, by one federal agency.
* * ♦ •
No Australian wines are on sale at the Imperial
Institute restaurants. The N.S.W. Government now
apparently partially realises the necessity of helping to
push the wine-industry, as the Department of Agriculture
pays the freight of 14 quarter-casks of N.S.W. wine now
being forwarded to Loudon as trade-samples.
