The smuggling jewellers are “Mistered ” by the
Argus—men who can aute-up thousand-pound fines, on
account of their swindles, are doubtless deserving of that
distinction. Jack Smith, who goes in for freetrade in
clothing by annexing a pair of trousers at the door of a
clothing-store, is dismissed Misterless to his hominy and
plank-bed, with a reflection on the depravity and intem-
perance of the working-classes. Seriously, why should a
man who robs the State he allowed to square the robbery
with a flue ? Why should the State compound what ought
to be felonies? If stolen money is found on the convicted
thief, it is judicially restored to the prosecutor ; but that
doesn’t save the thief from gaol! Why shouldn’t revenue-
robbers go to gaol ?

* * • »
Vic. butter exports for Eeb. this year almost
double the amount for the same month in '94—£121,000
against £62,000. This abnormal increase represents the
extraordinary growth of the trade. The total Australian
export now approaches in dimensions the Danish export.
It is inevitable that if the Australian rate of increase be
maintained much longer, a hopeless glut will ensue, and
the butter-trade will get on the shallows as the.wheat-
trade has done. Already a fall in London prices is an-
nounced.

The other day, a Melb. high-priestess of Venus,
urged, in vindication of her character as a financially-
honest woman, that on one occasion a bank-manager had
left £3OO at her place, which she had at once handed over
to the detective department. Who was that freehanded
financier? His name might afford a clue to the interpre-
tation, in at least one instance, of that vague item “gene-
ral expenses,” which loomed so large on every boom bal-
ance-sheet.
♦ * * •
The Chief Justice of Tasmania has ruled that
“ depreciation on the value of shares occuring in the fiscal
year may be deducted by taxpayers from their income
assessment, although the shares may not be actually sold
as stock.” Which reminds us of the Melb. merchant
who included among his reasons for going insolvent,
“depreciation in the value of my dwelling house and
grounds.” Tasmania seems to be a genial spot for the
bloated capitalist to roost upon. There he may form his
business into a limited liability co., retaining all the
shares in the family, and the family, by passing a few
shares from one to another at a depreciated price at the
end of each fiscal period, may escape payment of income-
tax every time. The same old depreciation will do ser-
vice from year to year, like the typical “converted" Jew.

The optimistic Pastor Eitohett intends to com-
memorate his presidency of the Wesleyans in Vic. and
Tas. by establishing a sinking-fund to work off the united
debt of £250,000. He will adopt Credit Fonoier methods.
Debentures, secured on the whole property, will be issued
at, say, 4J per cent. The difference between this rate
and the present rate (averaging 6 per cent) will be applied
to the sinking-fund.
* * * »
The Bank of N.S.W. takes on more junior
clerks in N.S.W. than the other banks put together. At
the beginning of this year an examination for junior
clerkships was established by Mr. Osbiston, the secretary
of the N.S.W. Bankers’ Institute. Since January, some 40
youths have notified they w'ouid present themselves for this
examination, but only 10 faced the ordeal. Eight of these
passed ; two of them receiving an appointment. The fee
for the examination is £l.
• * * *
At the meeting of Melb. Federal Coffee Palace
shareholders, last week, the chairman declared that as
long as the place was run merely as a teetotal con-
cern, the capital was hopelessly lost. The splendid pre-
mises cost £lBO,OOO to build and furnish, and the
directors can’t get a license. Last half-year’s business,
allowing for depreciation of assets, resulted in a loss of
£750, and there is not the least prospect of better things.
So the co., which is now £16,285 to the had, urges Govt,
to amend the Licensing Act by permitting an inferior
house to transfer its license, when the premises can be
run as a first-class hotel. If there is no simpler way out
of the difficulty, the Govt, shouldn’t hesitate—particu-
larly as the Savings Bank has a mortgage of £68,000 on
the building, and would find it a very inconvenient asset
to handle in case of forced foreclosure.
* • * »
Statistician Coghlan estimates that N.S.W.
Superannuation Fund has a deficiency of £2,905,199, if it
is to do all it promises. The fund was insolvent when it
started, and has been getting more insolvent ever since,
the deficiency now increasing at the rate of £120,000 a
year. The 4 per cent, contribution is inadequate, and
people who had no business to draw pensions have been
allowed £36,000 per annum. To make it solvent, the
promised allowances would have to be reduced by 65 per
cent. 1 As Govt, has obviously taken money from a large
number of civil-servants on contracts which it cannot
possibly carry out, the best thing to do is to cancel
the contracts, return contributors all moneys paid,
and merge the scheme in a State Life Assurance
Department on an actuarially sound basis. The
State Life Department must come, and Civil-service
assurance would form a good nucleus for it. Outside the
State there is no absolute security.

* » * *
The tributors have turned the tables on
lessees at the White Cliffs opal field. Formerly certain
lessees professed to suspect their tributors of annexing the
best stone ; latterly' some tributors have hinted, in a
vague, tantalising way, that certain bags have not seemed
as full as they were when handed over. If it was
only a ruse it answered admirably, and one hears much
less growling than formerly. There is at present an
exceptional demand for really good opal; but that class
of gem is scarce.
• * * •
In two months Central (8.H.) and Block 10
shares have advanced almost 100 per cent. More than
anything, the development at 1215 ft. in Ten has done
this. There they have a coarse-grained sulphide, going
about 250 z. silver and 25 per cent, lead, and anything
over 6ft. wide—for only one wall has been got yet.
* • * *
In about a week an ore-dressing machine,
designed by Banfield, an old Cornish dresser, will lie in
work at Broken Hill. The big mine people don’t give
much encouragement to outsiders with id as, but Banfield
has got the sympathies and cash of a good many Hill
trades-people (pessimists most of them where sulphides
are concerned), and will endeavour to run the thing as a
custom works. The sulphides he professes to find no
difficulty with.
* ♦ * •
One effect of lead-poisoning at Broken Hill is
noted by the Savings Bank officials. The wrist loses its
suppleness, and signatures change in a remarkable way.
Scores of orders are referred back to Sydney.
• * * ♦
The quiescence of Mt. Drysdale is simply the
history of many N.S.W. mines repeated. The share-
holders were largely local, and, to get divs., gouged the
show and worked it almost to a standstill as far as getting
out big quantities of ore was concerned. Now there is
dead work ahead. What the mine will he before the end
of the year no man knoweth, because it has been pig-
rooted, rather than worked. No machinery going up yet.
It will be time when they make sure they have a mine.

The Costerfield antimony mine (Vie.), now over
30 years old, has done nothing but put up and pull down
machinery throughout its long and futile existence. Bob
Beeston, the latest (twelfth or thirteenth) aspirant after
the unattainable—having much hurt himself in the pur-
suit—pronounces the successful concentration of anti-
monial sulphurets a Utopian delusion.

How intensely literary goldmining in Australia
is growing ! Louis Janin, Junr., incubating cyanide pro-
positions in Melb. ; Che exhaustless Eissler going to and fro
in N.S.W. seeking what he may scramble into “copy;”
and now O. G. Warnford Look, author of the mammoth
volume on “Practical Goldmining,” coming to succeed
W. J. Oollins in the management of “ Wentworth Pro-
prietary,” Lucknow.
By the way, there was a big deal and a huge
scoop in Wentworth shares the other day. The story
would make nice reading.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
The Bulletin has interviewed a miner just re-
turned from Westralia. He svys the local goldfields are the
best he ever met in an experience of 30 years : in under
two years he made a substantial pile— out of gimrerbeer.
When men gamble all night, it is usually the publican
only who wins. When men dig for gold, it is the store-
keeper who, in the end, gets most of that gold—if he only
lays to his book. If you want a dead certainty in this
world, don’t dig for gold. Make something to drink.
* ♦ * •
Things are not humming much yet at Marble
Bar, W.A., where, The Bulletin hears, there is a popula-
tion of 150. A man who was there the other day says that
when he looked into the two hotel billiard-rooms he found
both tables occupied by Chinamen.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
At Marble Bar, the Ironclad (Melbourne co.’s)
15-head battery near completion—7oo tons stone at grass
expected to crush well. Reef 4ft. in drive from North
and South shafts down 120 ft. on underlay.
» * • *
Mr. W. Nicholas has resigned bis appointment
as mining-lecturer at Melb. University after 17 years’ in-
valuable service, and returns to Westralia at once. The
University will find it hard to replace Mr. Nicholas, who
probably has a wider knowledge of Vic. mining, practi-
cally and theoretically, than anv other living man.
♦»‘ ♦ •
Premier Kingston (S.A.) is said to do well out
of the Isabella claim, lately floated in London ; but he
has an interest in what he believes to be a better mine
than that—the Elsie Conlifle, a White Feather property.
* • » *
The Westralian gold-companies promoted in
London up to the end of January numbered 81, with a
total capital of £7,618,400. Of these, as many as 52, re-
presenting £5,778,100, had been formed since the be-
ginning of October.
» • ♦ *
A Queenslander, writing from Westralia
“ Started off with three camels carrying a condenser up-
country. Arrived at our destination and found the
condenser too small. On our return journey found some
of Che soaks dry, and returned with one camel left alive
out of three.”
• * ♦ *
At last Ooolgardie shows a little confidence in
itself. The Moorefleld mine, three miles from the London-
derry, has been over-subscribed locally. Shaft 80ft.,
showing 3ft. reef with coarse gold—so runs the descrip-
tion. When Ooolgardie starts buying its own mines the
boom will be at its meridian.
* * * *
Londonderry shares quoted in London at 9s.
premium—caused, it is said, by the purchase of nearly all
the surrounding leases. The quietude of a graveyard and
the working of the Londonderry mine are synonymous.

La Nazione (Florence, Italy) in its issue of
January 7, under the heading, “An Enormous Treasure
in Australia ” :—“The gold-fever is increasing every day
in intensity in Australia, too. A certain Dann has dis-
covered a very rich mine at Ooolgardie which he has
christened‘The Wealth of Nations.’ In a few' days hs
has extracted ,£700,000 worth of pure gold, and the
extraction is increasing every day. A special detachment
of police has been sent from Adelaide to Ooolgardie to
protect this Thousand and One Night treasure. A Breton,
from Rheims, has discovered an auriferous deposit of
incalculable richness. He and his mates have refused to
sell the 1320 square feet of the mine to a company which
offered £lOOO for every inch, in all nearly 16 millions.
The explorers, however, suffer hardships supeiior to
human strength ; ,for one that succeeds hundreds
succumb. At Ooolgardie drinkable water costs 7J francs
the gallon, English measure, a little over one litre. Other
things cost in proportion.’’

A moan thing that commonly happens at Cool-
gardie; the destruction of letters entrusted to travellers
into town by' outside prospectors, the money given for
stamps being spent in liquor. Highway robbery is a bad
offence, but the bushranger is a refined and noble gentle-
man compared with the stamp-drinker.

The Bendigo-Coolgardie (near Mt. Burgess)
shares were saleable in Bendigo, w'here the shareholders
are, until they put up at enormous expense a 15-head
battery. Worse luck, too, they struck a body of water in
their deep shaft. One crushing was enough, and now',
when they are not crushing for the New Victoria, the
stampers are silent, and 20,000 gals. water per hour is
unused. Three more batteries will knock out 30 more
Ooolgardie mines. Truth lives in the stamp-mill.

Proclaimed that the decision to erect cyanide
works has caused a revolution at Pambula.
Events move rapidly at Ooolgardie. One week
it is telegraphed that there are 62 fever-patients in the
hospital and a greater number in private hospital-accom-
modation-houses outside ; the next, that the fever is
abating, and that there is no cause for alarm. The fact
remains that Ooolgardie is the greatest fever-bed in Aus-
tralia, and the chances are it will be worse before it is
better.

Dear Bulletin, —He 104 oz. nugget reported to
have been picked up on the surface of Hogan’s Gully,
Ooolgardie, some weeks back. The finder pegged off no
claim-did not even look to see if it had a mate.
The tucker-fossicker in the gully can’t point out where it
W’as found.—H.R.O.

Tasmania mine, 1308 tons for 1880 oz. gold.
This is one of the great gold-mines of Australasia. It has
turned out tons of gold, declared upwards of a million in
divs., and now, having complete control of a great body
of water, more tons of gold and numerous divs. may be
expected.

Ed. Bulletin, —He “Vosnibara,” No Trade
Protection Society ini Melb. has half-a-dozen inquiry
clerks, nor would such interruption to biz. be permitted as
the daily visit of such a number would entail. The lists
in these cutting days are a positive necessity to traders,
and are keenly studied. An official of one society is
member of a firm that has called a meeting of its
creditors ; now what sort of report has been given of the
status of that firm by the various T.P. Co.’s lor the last
few months? I insinuate nothing, for I know nothing—l
only remark that the situation must have been a delicate
one for at least one co. How is it that the clients of a
certain society were never warned about the big firms
which have lately sought the protection of the Insolvency
Court and have proved to have been insolvent for years?
Bluff is not achievement.—As Old Warrior.
» * * *
A note of the Provincial and Suburban Bank,
which burst disastrously in ’79, was passed the other day
on a Melb. publican who only realised his mistake on
trying to lodge the worthless paper in the bank. The
notes of Thomas Smith Richardson’s bank are still occa-
sionally cashed in Sydney, likewise those of other defunct
institutions. Hundreds of people don’t know the names
of half the note-issuing banks, and would cheerfully take
the paper of the Bank of Port Phillip, or any one of
half-a-dozen other vanished institutions. Another reason
for a State note-issue, so that the currency can’t be
inflated with dead men’s I O U’s.

What became of the expedition fitted out some
time ago in Melb., at a cost of £3OOO, to take up certain
alleged oil-country in the Northern. Territory ?
What between the influence of the depres-
sion, in keeping country customers at home, and
the influence of the coffee palaces in attracting
their patronage when they leave home, the trade
of many Sydney residential hotels is not now
what it used to be. Many persons, however, still
prefer the regularity of a good hotel to the hurry-
scurry of a coffee palace, and to these the old
Barley Mow (now wholly rebuilt), at the corner
of Park and Castlereagh streets, is recommended.
The accommodation is modern and ample, and the
proprietor, Mr. W- Macpherson, personally Sees
to the comfort of visitors.
A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who make*
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.

Personal Items.
Chief Justice Madden was as bald 20 years
ago as he is now.
Dr, Garran is the only journalist in N.S.W.
Legislative Council.
Says Australia’s richest man, James Tyson :
“I have never been at a race-meeting or in a
theatre in all my life.”
R. C. Bishop Torregiani, of Armidale, may
be regularly seen travelling on the N.S.W.
Northern line second class.
Lady Brassey brings to Victoria her
brother, “The Honorable Randolph De Vere
Capel.” That ought to knock ’em !
Parkes’ latest—his definition of “ a demo-
crat ” —is utterly unfit for publication. It is
clever, but, of course, it is not original.
A full-blooded Chinaman, Albert Wah
Sing, is a member of the Vic. militia. He is a
native of Creswick, Vic., and a cabinet-maker by
trade.
Premier Kingston is partly Irish. The
other day, he described his colleague, Gillen, as
“ a Paddy from the top of his feet to the sole of his
head.”
Some Tasmanian ladies presented Stoddart
with a cake on his recent birthday. He was sub-
sequently unable to play against Victoria through
indisposition.
Ah To Kong Sing, a bright young Chinese
half-caste, has hung out his shingle in Sydney as a
solicitor. He has Anglicised the first part of his
nam ; to Otto.
An Equity suit—-the extraordinary black -
and-gnld and knee-breeches rig-out worn by
N.S.W. Justice Owen at the swearing-in of the
Lieut.-Governor.
Despite the difference of the terms for
which they are nominally appointed, there are
more dead Governors in Australia than buried
Anglican bishops.
A Bulletin prophecy: “Nick” Fitz-
geral I, M. U.C. (Vic.), will be among the next
“Birthday” K.C.M.G.’s. The Ottawa Conven-
tion is the peg to hang the spurs on.
Justices Simpson, Windeyer, and Lines,
when boys, went together to the same Parramatta
school. Lately they sat together, similarly wigged
a id gowned, on Sydney Supreme Court Bench.
At Mudgee (N.S.W.) a colonist, John
Alden, who arrived in Governor Macquarie’s time
(1820), and is now said to be in his 106th year, is
awaiting transmission to the Liverpool (N.S.W.)
Asylum.
The meek and lowly Evangelist M‘Neil
explains, in reply to certain jibes, that he hired a
manger at the splendid Australia Hotel simply
because he couldn’t get parritch tit to eat in any
other Sydney hotel.
The Melb. “ society ” libel-case, Lange v.
Bige (plaintiff’s name is generally pronounced
Lang-ie) ought to be known as Slangey v. Rage,
considering the amount of bad temper and sloppy
English revealed by the evidence.
Chairman Fitzgibbon, of the Melb. Board
of Works, sent a telegram to Lady Duff on behalf
of the Board—so he told them at their next meet-
ing. Fitz.’s telegram ran thus :“ / beg to express
my earnest sympathy and condolence.”
The Oof Bird did not spread its wings
wide enough to tempt one of the greedy English
divines to the Deanery of Melb. or Bishopric of
Goulburn; but where the carcase of Adelaide is,
there are the vultures gathered together.
Freeman Thomas (Lord Brassey’s son-in-
law) is a cricketer of note. He has played against
three Australian Elevens in England—for Cam-
bridge University and Sussex. He was born in
Sept., 1866, and is therefore in his 29th year.
Fitchett told the Hobart Wesleyan Con-
ference that his literary work was “ put down
every day at God’s feet.” This conception of the
Deity as assistant-editor of Stead’s magazine of
clippings marks the lowest depth of pious bathos
yet reached.
A lady who has j ust figured as co-respondent
in a divorce-ease tried abroad, is reputed to have
a big say in the ownership of a certain Australian
daily, whose impassioned articles on the social
purity question are the despair of the clergy in
the opposition shop.
So far, there has been only one District
Court Judge promoted to N.S.W. Supreme Court
bench —the late Alfred Cheeke. Mr. Justice
limes was a D.C. Judge in Queensland, and Mr.
Justice Simpson in N.S. Wales, but both resigned
to resume practice at the Bar.
In ’B3, Dr. Stanton, then Bishop of N.
Queensland, was asked to act as referee to a
school which had successfully passed the first can-
didates prepared for Sydney University examina-
tions in N.Q. “The Church has nothing to do
with education,” replied the bishop.
W. P. Buckhurst, of Melbourne, who has
been several times around the world, says there is
only one place on earth where he couldn't get a
drink, and that was at Jericho. “So that,” he
says, “when you tell a man ‘to go to Jericho ’
you wish him more harm than you really know,”
It is not ujilikely that the new R.C. Bishop
if Port Augusta (S.A.), the see vacant by the
removal of Bishop O’Reilly to Adelaide, will
be Father Norton, of Petersburg, a Ballarat boy,
who served his time as an architect to G. R.
Johnson, and helped to draw the plans for Melb.
Theatre Royal.
Dr. Nicholson, Bart., who, according to
last mail accounts, figured at the Victoria Insti-
tute, London, in the discussion on Australian
botany, enjoys the unique privilege of having
filled the office of Speaker in both N.S.W. and
Queensland Legislative Assemblies. He is now
in his 87th year.
Vic. Statistician Hayter, one of the
brightest ornaments of the Australian Civil Ser-
vice, who died last week at 74, was in harness
almost to the last. He had been 38 years in the
Govt, service, and 11 years Vic. Govt. Statistician.
A portrait of Mr. Hayter and notice of his useful
and honorable life, appeared in The Bulletin
some months ago.
Father Tuck well, lately appointed to a
chaplaincy away in the Pyrenees, the mountain
boundary between France and Spain, was long
well-known in Manly as the “double” facially
and in carriage of the late Archbishop Vaughan.
His reverence, after leaving Melb. Custom House,
went to Europe and received ordination at the
now famous and Zolaesced Lourdes. A few
months ago he was transferred from Redfern to
Granville, where one of the late storms blew the
roof off his church.
Queensland seems to have disagreed with
most of its Governors, George Bowen, its first
ruler (1859), and Norman (its present) being the
only two of its seven Governors left alive. The
others were Blackall, Normanby, Cairns, Ken-
nedy, and Musgrave. N.S. Wales has, perhaps,
the longest list of ex-Governors living—Belmore,
Robinson, Loftus, Carington, and Jersey. Vic-
toria has Barkly, Bowen, Loch and Hopetoun;
while South Australia has the oldest living ex-
Viceroy, George Grey, who, 53 years ago, ruled in
Adelaide.
James Whiteside M‘Cay, of Castlemaine,
was the other day rated LL.B. of Melb. Univer-
sity, after having obtained first-class honors in
every year, without a break. The exhibition,
£2OO, was awarded to him this year. He is about
the only man, except Isaacs, who has done so
brilliant a course in Vic. M‘Cay is quite a pro-
minent politician in Castlemaine, and has strong
democratic leanings. It was principally at his
instance, and by his support, that E. D. Williams
came out against Patterson at last election, Pat-
terson being relegated to second place on the poll.

A wretched little denominational print,
Victorian Churchman, descends to the very
depths of meanness in its current issue. Lord
Rosebery has conferred a civil-list pension on the
widow of the late Dr. Pearson, scholar, historian,
journalist, and Radical politician, on account of
her husband’s distinguished literary and political
services. The Churchman waxes indignant at
this because Pearson Was “ practically a pro-
nounced Agnostic,” and also because of “ his
opposition to the introduction of Scriptural lessons
into our Public-schools.” It is oidy a professional
Christian paper which could descend to the loath-
some meanness of jealously grudging the payment
of a small sum to a widow, on account of its objec-
tion to the political work of her husband in his
lifetime.
The Duchess of Leinster died last week,
and the cable-man sent the sad news along, as
though Australia took an interest in the lady. It
happened that Australia was remotely interested
quite unbeknown to itself. The Duchess’s brother
married Alice Leamar’s sister—and separated
from her in due season.
There is no sign yet of a bust or painting
of Chief Justice Higinbotham, the noblest Roman
of them all, being placed in the Melb. Law Courts
library, where Justices Mules worth, Fellows, and
Barry, and Chief Justice A’Beckett are com-
memorated. C. J. Stawell is in the picture
gallery, as an ex-Acting Governor.
There has been a squabble among the
three “ experts ” appointed to enquire why Vic.
railways don’t pay, and one of them, Mr. Cain
(a former Mayor of Melbourne), says he’ll resign.
The other day Premier Turner, meeting in the
street Judge Casey, one of the commissioners,
asked “How about Cain?” “How should I
know ?” replied Casey ; “ I’m not my brother’s
keeper.”

courts the course of justice is very summary. In
obscene-language cases one magistrate insists on
the arresting constable reciting on oath the exact
words used. The other day a constable much de-
murred, but the magistrate was firm. “ Well,
your worship, the accused said that the
(meaning your worship) was the worst drunkard
in ” “Three months,” said the beak.

Premier Reid’s use
of an eyeglass is no mere
affectation, as was shown
the other day in N.S.W,
Full Court, on the occa-
sion of the installation as
a Judge of ex-Attorney-
General Simpson. It is
ordinarily the custom for
the junior Judge to sit
at the extreme left of the
bench, but on this occa-
sion the Judges had, as a
compliment, seated the
new Judge on their ex-
treme right. Mr. Reid,
devoid of his eye-glass,
proceeded to address his
congratulations to the
left of the bench, where
sat Mr. Justice Manning;
but, later on, a barrister
having nudged the
speaker and explained
matters to him, he aimed
the rest of his oration at
the right quarter.
David Syme has issued an edict that the
Argus is not to be alluded to as “ our contem-
porary.” David believes the Argus to be half-a-
century behind the Age.
George Ash, of whom a portrait is here
printed, is one of the youngest, and at the same
time one of the most interesting figures in the
S.A. Parliament. Though not yet 40, he
served in the Cape Volunteer Force during
the Kaffir War of 1877. He was then a
journalist, and became . a soldier because the
Commandant refused him permission to go to
the front in any other capacity. He came
to S.A. shortly after the conclusion of the
campaign, and in ’BO, in conjunction with J. B.
Mather, now artist on the staff of the Adelaide
Advertiser, obtained possession of the Narra-
coorte Herald, the price agreed to be paid for the
paper being .£3OOO, He worked very hard as
editor, and after nine years’ labor "had just
managed to pay off the indebtedness to the
firm from which the Herald was purchased.
He also had acquired a house and seventeen
acres of land, on which his expenditure in
money was more than ,£l3OO. In April
1889, the Herald published strictures on th >
e
conduct of a local squatter, and the biggest libel-
action ever heard in S.A. resulted. It lasted
more than three weeks, despite the refusal o(
Judge Buuoaut to admit the evidence of 40 wit-
nesses subpoenaed to testify to the facts allege(l in
the article complained of. In the end a verdict
for plaintiff for £5250 was given by 9 votes against
Photo, by Frihling pnd Co.] [Adelaide.
GEORGE ASH , M.P.
3, the minority capitulating to save trouble.
The costs exceeded .£2OOO. Mr. Ash con-
ducted his own case with great ability, and
won considerable popularity by his courage and
resource. Nevertheless he and his partner left
the court ruined men. Time to realise the estate
and pay verdict and costs was refused. The
home which had cost Mr. Ash .£l7OO was sold for
.£240, and his share in tiie paper was sacrificed in
similar fashion. The Insolvent Court followed
the libel-case, all Ash’s furniture being also swal-
lowed. “I was literally without a penny,” said
Mr. Ash to the writer, “and my wife and I had
to live chiefly on rabbits for three months.” In
compliance with a requisition from the people
of Albert the constituency in which the
Herald had been conducted —he stood for
the electorate at the next general election
(April, 1890), all the money at his command
to meet living and Campaign expenses being
a sum subscribed by admirers and presented to
his wife. On April 9, 1890—exactly a year after
the publication of the article which caused the
libel-action—Mr. Ash was returned at the top of
the poll for Albert. He saw that he could not
live independently on the .£2OO a year Parlia-
mentary pay, and at the end of the first session he
determined to study for the law. Mr. Kingston,
Q.C. (now Premier) offered to give him his articles
if he passed matriculation, and Ash bought the
necessary books and set to work, and for eight
weeks stuck to his studies for 14 hours daily.
He was successful, to the surprise of everyone, and,
finishing Ids examination on the Friday, he began
his law-course on the following Monday. During
his University career he twice took the highest
scholarships in law at the end of successive terms,
and on Dec. 19,1894, took the Bachelor of Laws
degree, being the first student to qualify under
the new regulations. Directly afterwards he was
admitted into partnership with the Premier
(Kingston, Q.C.). It is significant that while Ash
passed in 54'separate papers during his University
career and spent 119 J hours in answering the
questions, the only query put by the examiners to
Kingston, Q.C.—when he qualified for admission
years ago—was “ What will you take to drink ?”
This being replied to tactfully, he was
allowed to practice. Ash in Parliament has won
a reputation for indomitable perseverance, utter
consistency, bull-dog courage, and kindness of
heart. In many things, he is ultra-conservative.
He is a freetrader and a tireless advocate for the
opening of Crown lands for settlement. Many a
squatter has had to giveupcoveted leases becauseof
his agitation. Though in the past an articled-clerk
and now a partner of Kingston, he is a thoroughly
independent member, and the most caustic criti-
cism of Government measures has often come
from him. He votes against the Ministry, too,
and if there is a no-confidence motion when the
House meets, will probably help to turn the
Government out. Many are the skirmishes which
have taken place between him and the Labor-
party, who dislike him intensely—and it is pro-
phesied that, as most of the S.A. village-
settlements have been established in Albert,
he will be dismissed from the representation of
the district next year in favor of a less rigid
individualist. He was the first S.A. legislator to
definitely and decidedly decline to subscribe to
football clubs and other similar district institu-
tions for extorting money from politicians, but he
makes a point of answering every letter written to
him by a constituent, and his correspondence is
tremendous. He is always busy either writing or
reading in the House, and in attendance and in
his votes on divisions is exemplary. He speaks
well and has a bright fund of humor.
HOT CLIMATES.—“I cannot refrain from
adding my testimony to the great efficiency of your
‘ FRUIT SALT ’ here. I have a store, and as
numerous travellers are constantly passing, and
the climate being extremely hot, frequently 110° in
the shade, there is naturally much drink consumed.
It is a colonial custom that if a traveller drops in
your store, to ask the proprietor to ‘liquor;’ I did
‘liquor’ for some considerable time, and found
my liver gradually increasing in consequence.
Having been recommended your excellent ‘FKUIT
SALT,’ I steadily applied myself to six bottles in
accordance with the instructions given, and I feel
myself bound to say that the result was not only
satisfactory, but, I believe, has permanently re-
duced my liver to its normal size. Since then I
invariably use your ‘FRUIT SALT ’ when asked
to ‘liquor’ in place of the beverage which in-
ebriates and doesn’t always cheer, and charge the
customer as per usual. I remain, Sir, yours truly,
W; Schmidt, Barkly Rout, Griqualand West,
South Africa. J. C. End, Esq.” Sold by all
Chemists and Stores.

POLITICAL
POINTS
There are four legal firms represented in
the Turner Ministry (Vic.).
Sydney dailies now Keep standing the
heading, “ Another Exp’anation by Mr. Lyne.”
, General Hutton : “Australia has before
her a great military future, and her soldiers realise
that responsibility.” Save us from our defenders !
It is generally understood that W. H.
Suttor’s resignation of the Vice-Presidentship of
N.S.W. Executive is due to his disapproval of
Premier Reid’s Land-Tax proposals.
Victorian parsons who decry Secularism
are Secularists to a rnan in the matter of the State
undertaking the religious education of the young.
They are too lazy to do it themselves.
A few months ago the Vic. selectors’
arrears were only .£500,000. On the publication
of this, all hands determined not to pay any more,
and so the arrears are now =£750,000.
Several Queensland M.’sL.A. pay far more
than the amount of their Parliamentary salaries
to substitutes to conduct their businesses during
the time they are in Brisbane attending Parlia-
ment.
The Payment of Members Bill proposed
by the Rosebery Government is to be a payment
out of the rates of each electorate, thus coming
within the sphere of payment Parkes and Dibbs
occasionally favor.
The backbone of the present Sydney city-
railway agitation consists of certain individuals
who have on hand big and unprofitable Circular
Quay land-specs., and who somehow never talk
or write betterment tax.
Nearly every one of the Vic. ex-Ms. P.
who have, so far, omitted to surrender their Par
liamentary railway-passes, belongs to the Tory side
of the House, and was formerly a blatant bar-
racker for the defeated Patterson.
“What status had the self-constituted
‘Conference of Premiers ’ aerainst the great his-
torical Convention ? pan the lesser overrule the
greater ? Can a coterie of mice claim for itself the
mastery over a gathering of lions ?”-— Parkes.
The State itself must directly conduct the
grog-traffic. A Sydney publican fined for selling
brandy unfit for human consumption pleaded that,
save for the admixture of water, he sold the liquor
just as he got it from the wholesale dealer. Who
can possibly decide between the merchant and the
publican ? To place the matter beyond doubt all
temptation to adulterate must be removed. The
State alone would have no such temptation.
In a week or two,
the only Governor on
the Australian conti-
nent will be General
Norman, of Q’sland.
N.S.W. Legisla-
tiveOouncil has killed
another democratic
proposal — the pro-
posal to reform the
Marine Board by
allowing several re-
presentative bodies to
nominate one mem-
ber each. At present
four members are
supposed to be nomi-
nated by Govt., and
three by the ship-
owners; but the ship-
owners practically
nominate the lot.
Hence the difficulties
thrown in the way of
public redress in cases
of overcrowding,
under-manning, and
deficient provision of
boats and safety ap-
pliances. The accom-
modation provided in
many boats which
have left Sydney for
Westralia and Maori-
land has been simply
scandalous; yet a
deputation of ship-
ping companies had
the impudence the
other day to ask Pre-
mier Reid to further
reduce their responsi-
bilities. The N.S.W.
shipping laws will
bear a lot of improve-
ment, as soon as a
Government can be
found able and willing
to improve them.
It is believed there has been a row in the
Kingston Ministry because of the appointment of
a Commission to enquire into the Adelaide hospital
dispute. The trouble arose because of the pro-
motion of Chief Secretary Gordon’s sister over
the heads of a number of senior nurses. The
Govt, confirmed the appointment while Premier
Kingston was away in Hobart, and, the public
clamoring, he insisted upon an independent in-
vestigation of the matter when he returned.
A commentary upon the Archbishop of
Canterbury's assertion as to the godless and sinful
character of young Victorians (based on clerically-
garbled statistics flatly contradicted by Hayter)
is the fact that, owing to the dearth of crimin-
ality, Govt, is reducing the official staffs at
country gaols.
“ When we approach the province of
nation-making it is difficult for Premier Reid, and
men who grope in his narrow track of thought,
to comprehend the situation. We might as well
expect a guinea-pig to take a correct survey of the
stars. The poor guinea-pig can never rise to the
occasion. ” — Parkes.
N.S.W. ex-Minister Lyne’s wail over the
existence of the Labor-party is piteous. “They
wanted two clearly-divided parties to secure the
continued existence of Party Government, which
was the heart and soul of public life.” Two’s
company and three’s none! The Gooseberry-
party of politics—the Labor-party.
Premier Reid has already added two mem-
bers to N.S.W, Legislative Council. They are
officeholders in the Government, of course, but
still they are Reid appointments to the Council,
and, if they resign, one the Vice-Presidency and
the other the Attorney-Generalship, both may
continue to sit in the Council for life.
The Vic. Naval Brigade was recently mar-
shalled before the new Commandant under strict
injunctions to wear no gaiters. In view of the
absence of war-clouds from the Age cables, gaiters
have been put on a peace footing, but the enemy
need not flatter itself. Though Victoria’s gaiters
have joined the reserves, they are ready for action.
He was a Bananaland M.L.C. who sim-
mers in continual indignation at the scandal of
payment of members. A few weeks after one of
his fiery diatribes against this iniquity he wrote
to the Railway-Commissioners, enquiring if his
position as legislator entitled him to have wood
and water carried free on the public railway to his
suburban residence.
As to the fraudulent and time-expired
free-pass question, why, The Bulletin again
asks, don’t the various Australian railway-depart-
ments change yearly the metal of which passes are
made? Last year’s pass say, of engraved silver ;
this year’s of engraved copper. A square pass
last year, a triangular one this year. Then there
could be no mistake.
“ We began writing for it (the eight-hours
principle) 40 years ago,” says the Argus. Next
century that paper will be boasting how it was one
of the first to make popular the principles of
Anarchy, and how it was a member of the Argus
staff that invented the patent Death-to-Fat-
Men bomb whicli secured such privileges for
the Australian working-man.
“ If we give Justices of the Peace enor-
mous powers over the liberties of the people—
powers in some respects far higher than those
given to the Judges of the Supreme Court, who
can sentence a man to imprisonment only after he
has been convicted by a common jury—it would
be only reasonable to take some steps to ascertain
that justices know the law they administer.
Justices of the Peace should be required to prove
by certificate of examination that they are
acquainted with the rudiments of the law whieh
they have to administer. For my part, I should
very much desire to see unpaid justices done awa >-
with altogether. Higinbotham.

REGENT TEST-MATCH.
ENGLISH JOHNNIE : “ How is it that wheahevah an Englishman goes
he is always an Englishman ?”
AUSTRALIAN ACQUAINTANCE : “ Well, I suppose on the same prin-
ciple that wherever an ass goes he is always an ass.”
At latest, Henniker Heaton was address-
ing an enthusiastic meeting of his Canterbury
Tory constituents, and swearing that the Conser-
vative members would never handle a penny of
the proposed parliamentary pay, but would “ pass
it over to the sick and aged.” That sounds well,
but it is only the sound of the fall of Dives’ crumbs.
The vulgar Labor-member Lazarus has not so
many sausages on his table that he can afford to
sweep them off with Ins elbows.
Apropos of Deakin’s lecture on Liberalism,
Melb. Argus says: “ He fancies himself a new
species of Supreme Being.” Well, that is a harm-
less conceit common to moat men, and to none so
much as Mr. Haddon, who sub-edits the Al-
mighty’s copy every evening, and daily prints his
Fat Man’s version of the Universe “ in the place
where I am demanded by conscience to speak
the truth.”
Melb. journalist: “The Argus keeps
going down, down, down in public estimation.
What it ought to do is to cut under the Age for
the support of the masses, but, unfortunately, it is
choked with the fossilarians, who haven’t had
time to die out. Meanwhile, the Age has
retreated again to its Conservative attitude, from
which it was frightened a little by the Argus
tumble to a penny. The Conservative supporters
of the Age stick to it, because thus both papers
are collared.”
THE LOST CHANCE MINE.
ARTURNED W.A. PROSPECTOR (to his wife):
“Maria, what have you done with my
clothes ?”
WIFE :“ Washed them !”
PROSPECTOR (in despair) : “ Another fortune
gone ! I was going to dry-blow them. There
was three months’ Kurnalpi dust in ’em.”
Dr. Garran surrendered office in N.S.W.
Legislative Council to take the Presidency of the
Conciliation and Arbitration Board. In due time
that Board was abolished, and now Dr. Garran is
“elevated back” to his old position. Garrard
has an Amended Trades Disputes Conciliation
Bill now before the Assembly, and when this Bill
reaches the Council it will naturally, from Dr.
Garran’s past experience of the question, fall
to him to nurse and defend. If it becomes
law, will Dr. Garran be “elevated down” and
“promoted back” to the stipendiary Presidency
of the Board once more ?
The Argus has discovered that one man
out of every 400 in Victoria was convicted of
cheating the Customs last year, and thinks this a
strong argument in favor of a return to Freetrade.
According to this, if one person out of 200 were
legally convicted annpally of some offence, there
would be 100 per cent, more reason for abolishing
laws altogether than there is for instituting Free-
trade. Freetrade is wanted, in the interests of
morality, to abolish smugglers; therefore, free
love is wanted to abolish bigamists; also, if
there were no coins there would be no coiners,
for which reason the monetary system is iniquitous
and must be wiped out. The Argus, the organ
of Anarchy, one penny, all news-agents.
The whirligig of time. Melb. Argus
supported the candidature of Broguey Man
O’Loghlen at last Carlton bye-election, yet this
is the notification which he published in the Go-
vernment Gazette of 20th Feb,, 1879, during his
tenure of office as Vic. Premier
NOTICE.
In consequence of the fabrication by and the publica-
tion in the Argus journal of false news, purporting to be
genuine and authoritative, concerning the proceedings
and discussions of the Cabinet, being continuously per-
severed in for several weeks last past (sic), the public are
hereby cautioned against giving any credence to either
those statements, or any similar kind of news tor the
future in the journal. The Argus has been refused any
official information of the kind by the acting Chief Secre-
tary, who feels justified in the taking this course, as that
journal has for several months unpatriotically attempted
to depreciate the financial credit of Victoria.
Bryan O’Loghlen,
Chief Secretary’s Office, Acting Chief Secretary.
Melb., 20th Feb., 1879.
Premier Reid simply paralysed Parkes
when the latter, last week, with a view to pro-
voking a crisis, moved the adjournment of N.S.W.
Assembly to “draw attention to the confusion
into which the Government has plunged the coun-
try ” —though he (Parkes) had ju t voted against
Dibbs’motion to a similar effect. Georgey-Porgey
Reid is a! ways good at a back-answer, but he lias
rarely, if ever, reached the virulence, neatness,
and dramatic intensity of retort which he exhibited
on this occasion. His squeaky voice was raised to
its shrillest pitch as he pointed to the Opposition
benches, and told the played-out king of the Kiama
ghosts: “Your place is there! We want no
traitors in the Freetrade' camp.” The Minis-
terialists cheered again and again, while the Oppo-
sition sat and enjoyed the fun, and the old man
impotent couldn’t even muster strength to reply.
It is really time the fallen idol retired from
politics and devoted himself exclusively to corres-
pondence with “Mister” Gladstone,
The Yic. President of Melb. Hardware
Association complained to Vie. Tariff Board that
the trade is demoralised every June in anticipa-
tion of the Treasurer’s Budget. Protection, the
nn-settled policy of the country, has many things
to contend with, but one of the worst is tariff-
tinkering. A community may accommodate itself
to even a bad tariff, but the wavering policy that
ventures forward in one session and retrogresses in
panic the next, is a monstrous libel on the policy
of Protection. A tariff should not be a job-lot of
mixed taxes —it should be formulated with care
and maintained with firmness.
Bryan O’Loghlen, speaking at Carlton
before the election, propounded a characteristically
O’Loghlenian scheme for the restoration of pros-
perity. First of all, the Govt, was to lend the
reconstructed banks several millions for the pur-
pose of creating “ confidence,” secondly, the afore-
said banks were to lend several millions (presum-
ably the same) to the Govt, with the same laudable
object. Then things were to begin booming, and
the old infallible receipt of borrowing £20,000,000
and spending it was to follow. The Broguey
Man is, as a rule, a startlingly original financier,
but this latest proposal is obviously borrowed from
the two Irishmen in the yarn who bought a keg of
whisky and proceeded to make a fortune out of
retailing it at sixpence a drink to one another.
The raw-boned offspring of Parry-Okeden,
Q. Under-Col, Secretary, Was, the other day,
quietly appointed Aoting-Sub-Inspector of
Police at <£2oo a year, while scores of capable
tnen of double his years already in the
service plead vainly for promoting. It is
hardly three years since this youngster
left Brisbane Grammar School, and after
a couple of years in a subordinate place
in the Q.N. Bank, Ije was jumped into
his present position. He gets the billet,
so far as can be judged by the public,
simply because he is/the son of his father,
who was thought to have done good ser-
vice to Government as magistrate in
charge of the disturbed districts during
the recent bushworkers’ strike. Mean-
while, a former high Ipcal military
official —scholar, officer, and gentleman
was taking admis'sion - tickets for a
living at the last match the English
cricketers played in Brisbane. There
is nobody in the province better
qualified for the billet dropped into
callow Okeden’s beak.- But, in Kana-
kaland, kissing goes by father.

“I doubt,” says a Bulletin correspon-
dent, “ whether there is any real necessity for
paying Australian Governors. If you secretly
auctioned the N.S.W. Governorship in London
you would get high bids from a thousand ambitious
snobs.”
Guildford (W.A.) licensing bench was
“knocked endways” the other day by receipt of
a telegram from one of the Forrest gang, saying
“ he objected ” to the granting of a certain license
applied for. Whether this was intended. as. evi-
dence, or as an attempt to browbeat the Bench on
behalf of a competing publican, doesn’t appear,
but the incident shows the colossal impudence of
W.A. magnates. The Bench granted the license,
A standing curse of S. Australia is the
“Royal Commission ” business, a device of needy
politicians to make hay in summer outside their
salaries. The N. Territory Commission has just
decided to visit Queensland in April to enquire
into the labor question, and the S.A. taxpayer
will have to foot a bill for .£2OOO or .£3OOO
to provide a health trip for politicians, while he
himself is wearing patched clothes and living on
two meals a day. The coolie labor-question is as
dead as Julius Caesar in S.A., and a while ago the
Assembly decided against alien labor being em-
ployed in the N.T.; but Commissioners never lack
gall to invent a barefaced device for a cheap holi-
day. A trip to Sydney or Hobart to enquire into
the price of fish "would have been quite as bene-
ficial.
In supporting the N.S.W- Medical Prac-
titioners’ Bill, which makes all practitioners state
clearly their qualifications, G. R. Dibbs stated
that a Sidney quack-doctor, who had been a
boatswain, “boasted to him that he had made a
fortune of <£150,000 by the practice of medicine,
and that it had cost him from <£l7oo to <£2ooo a
year in fighting this bill. He (Dibbs) sent this
person to the Minister for Justice, to whom he re-
peated his statements.” The second reading of
the bill was carried by 52 votes to 14, and The
Bulletin is sorry to see the minority composed
chiefly of Labor members. Every man Jack of
those Labor members would to-morrow vote for a
bill for branding Chinese-made furniture. Why
shouldn’t a quack-doctor have to brand his goods?
What injustice would be done if every man had to
brand his goods ?

Sydney Dairy Teeegraph solemnly began
its commercial article the other day :
The mortal nature of the Governor’s illness was not
generally understood until the afternoon, and a slow but
fairly steady business was reported. The report for the
entire week is on very similar lines. The turnover has
not at any point been active, but the tone has been good,
and prices well supported. On the Stock Exchange, &c.
Which is an even more beautiful anti-climax than
a passage from the D.T.’s eloquent description of
the gubernatorial funeral-procession :
Ahead, between the trees, the hearse and the flower-
decked gun-carriage, leading a long line of cabs.
A long line of cabs is indeed a romantic and
solemn circumstance. Then again :
It (the grave) is an ideal resting place. On one side lie
the remains of the late Lord Ancram, on the other those of
the late R‘v. Alex. Milne Jarvie, whilst the vaults of the
families of Sir F. M. Darien and Sir Georje Innes are
in the immediate vicinity
Then the D.T. tells us that “slowly the sun sank
in the West”—as if on days when Governors
were not buried it sank in the Bast at 60 miles an
hour ! Australia will never be a Republic, will
never be anything but what it is—an old-young
people with a rising generation of boys growing
up on cheap cigarettes an i unoriginal blasphemy,
and girls on cheek and rice-powder—so long as
such stuff is published.
The S.M. Herald was equally idiotic. On
two days it mourned with upturned column-rules
over four of its pages. Why it didn’t mourn over
the remaining pages i in’t clear. As it was, the
shipping news was in a very bad way over Gov.
Dulf’s death, while the theatrical and commercial
columns couldn’t squeeze a tear. Much obvious
woe, also, was slobbered over the “lost and
found ” advts., and the “situations wanted,”
while the “auction sales” and money-lending
announcements were quite unmoved. Bub the
abject, crawling, horrible bathos of the page
devoted to an account of poor Gov. Duff’s death
more than atones for these superficial discrepan-
cies. To read it is to squirm with disgust.
* * *
S.M. Herald gubernatorial-funeral en-
thusiasm :
The Principal Under Secretary’s first step was to com-
mit to Mr. O. Kinsela administrative details. Mr. Kinsela,
in his turn, whilst guarding his staff from the preparation
of anything that would be at all ostentatious, laid it down
that everything was to be incomparably perfect.
Elsewhere we read that “the coffin was lined
with swansdown ” —no doubt the undertaker’s
idea of incomparable perfection. Ugh ! After
this the story of the old lady who died last week
at Melbourne, and whose body was, at her own
request, burnt to ashes on a wood-pile by the sea,
the lighting match being applied by her son,
comes as a breezy relief.
* * *
In Melbourne Criminal
Court, the other day, a cer-
tain dreary old barrister
begged the jury to send the
prisoner home to his wife
and children without a stain
on his character, or, at any-
rate, to recommend the
family bread-winner to
mercy for the sake of his
dear ones. The aforesaid
prisoner was a bachelor, as
it happened. “ Remember,
you are on your oath,"’
thunders learned counsel at
the respectable witness in
the box, yet the bewigged

There is a mild uproar
in Melbourne tooth-pulling

College idea. Now that a

That sea s'iorecremation —the first in Victoria—
was, however, not an unqualified success, for at
one stage the skull of the deceased showed out
and some wood thrown over it caused it to break
and expose the brain. The crudeness of this
attempt should stimulate St. Kilda Cemetery
trustees to quickly provide their crematorium,
as repetition would tend to prejudice the public
against the rational disposal of the dead.
# * *
Half-moon Bay, where this rough-and-
ready cremation occurred, is a picnic resort, and
an audience soon assembled to discuss the burning
question and give advice. Meanwhile, a small
picnic-party not far off was placidly devouring its
victuals and drinking beer out of a billy, and a
knob of fishermen standing near were making
anxious enquiries as to whether deceased wore any
rings or other valuables. The remains were not
collected, but the relatives and friends of deceased
intimated their intention of making pilgrimages
to the spot. If they go soon they need not be sur-
prised to find an enterprising fisherman, with a
cradle and tin dish, fossicking among the ashes.
♦ * *
The Sandringham (Vic.) people are ob-
jecting to cremations being performed there in the
present fashion. They have now had two within
a week.
» * •
Apropos several recent dinners. To give
a banquet in a man’s honor is the greediest form
of compliment. Like “the quality of mercy,”
“ it blesseth him that gives and him that takes.”
The giver gets as much out of it as the getter. It
is a kind of a co-operative compliment, in which
the cornplimenters are partners, and the compli-
raentee gets no more than a free meal and a suc-
cession of hiccupy speeches. Giving public dinners
to people is just as selfish as sending advertise-
ments —we mean ticketed wreaths —to their
funerals.
By the way, two days after Gov. Duff’s funeral,
lists of people who had attended the funeral, but
whose names had been omitted from the first news-
paper-lists, appeared in the Sydney dailies. It
will interest the public to know that, with hardly
an exception, the persons whose names were re-
corded in these second lists, wrote in to the news-
paper offices asking that their names should lie
printed. And so, for that matter, did a large
number of the people whose names were in the
first lists. For such is the immodesty of the pre-
tentious humbug and pompous self-advertising
nobody.
# * *
A coming sensation is a divorce-suit in
which a well-connected wealthy Englishman will
figure as co-respondent. It is alleged that the
husband had his wife “shadowed,” and on her
return home one night she was refused admittance.
To express her indignation as forcibly as possible
she proceeded to break all the windows in hubby’s
domicile, and was only removed by main force in
the person of two policemen, to the great delecta-
tion of a fashionable neighborhood. Madame’s
petits sou per s (during her husband’s absence) are
vividly remembered by a large number of gilded
youth.
# # *
Tha “Vagabond” suggests the intro-
duction of a co-operative kitchen system into
Australia. What a chance for professional philan-
thropy to form a syndicate and make money by
well-doing ! A co-operative kitchen would mean
a return of good temper and good looks to the
average poor man’s wife, who now divides her
time between cooking and cleaning, and cursing
the hour when she got married.

“Fritz” Lange’s allegation of unfair treat-
ment at Oxford University on account of his
being an Australian, which was adduced at the
trial as evidence of insanity, may not be absolutely
groundless. The young fellow is apparently a
boy of the most bigotedly patriotic views, and not
at all one to accept the superior airs of the scions
of old nobility. Young Lange’s undisguised lack
of admiration for University rowing would not be
likely to increase the popularity at Oxford.

Those 200 goats had not, at latest, shown
up at Goolgardie, where the goat population so far
consists of but two, whose milk is being retailed
to fever-patients at a shilling a, gill. New-laid
eggs are very hard to get at Is. apiece.
* * •
Coolgardie hospital, writes a correspon-
dent, consists of seven Hessian tents, containing
GO beds. The largest ward, 30ft. x 15ft. holds
twelve, and is called the private, or paying, ward,
and the charges are £4 a week, of which £2 10s.
goes to the hospital and the balance to the doctor.
As this ward was erected and furnished .at the
expense of the public, is It right that an indigent
patient should be refused admittance when all the
beds in the other wards are occupied and there is
room in the paying ward ?

Too woomba and Rock-
hampton have earned un-
enviable notoriety for the
severity of their juries. It
was Rockhampton that
found a Mount Morgan
Chinese guilty of homicide
on practically no evidence
at all—a decision set at
naught by the Cabinet,
which liberated the Chow
within 18 months. Rock-
hampton juries always do
their duty by the Boodle-
wraith in political trials.
It is easily understood,
however. A juror cooped
up in a crowded den for
many hours at a lime, with
the thermometer 120 de-
grees in the refrigerator,
must have someone’s blood.
No man ought to be tried
for life by a jury locked-up
in a camp-oven.

A Melb. “ society”
paper tells “ Lassie ” that
she may go and see her
Gnssie, who is ill in bed :
“ Put on your most becom-
ing garments; if possible,
wear a tiny spray of his
favorite flower, and carry
into the sick room your
very brightest smile, even
should your heart ache and
your courage well nigh fail.
Do not object to the pre-
sence of the nurse, and, at
a sign from her, at once say
goodbye to your loved one,
leaving with him the sweet
white carnations you had
worn and the memory of
your loving smiles, to com-
fort him till ‘to-morrow.’ ”
Ain’t it sickenin’ ?
The awfulness of a marine catastrophe is measured, in
the daily press, by the rank and station of the victims.
Take two recent headings from Melb. Argus— “ Wreck of
the Schooner Martha. Capsized in the Rip. All on
board drowned." “Shocking Boating Accident near
Perth. Four well-known men drowned." The Age
announcements were—“ Capsized in the Rip. A Tas-
manian Schooner Founders. Three Lives Lost ’’ ; and
“Terrible Yachting Fatality. Four-gentlemen drowned
at Perth.”—
Two different Melb. establishments adver-
tise “medical advice and medicine, 2s. 6d.,”and
most of the very best suburban men are now quite
content with a ss. fee for consultation. Times
have changed ! Twenty years ago any sober,
decently-qualified young medico could land in
Australia without a cent, settle down almost at
once to a good practice in the country, marry a
squatter’s daughter, and, if he kept sober, retire
with a fortune in ten years. Now there are six
red lamps in every street and one under nearly-
every gum-tree. But still there is room —at the
top. One consultant with a London reputation
who arrived in Sydney without beat of drum less
than two years ago is probably making .£4OOO a
year, and never talks to anybody under two
guineas.

WORTH.
He died, and through the world the tidings ran;
Fame heard, and set apart a lofty niche :
And did he feed the hungry poor—this man?
Nay—more than that—he clothed the naked
rich !
The Martha was a trading ship,
Her sailors rough and browned—
She met with weather in the Rip
And all aboard were drowned.
Their wives and children sob at night,
Such grief we cannot share,
The Martha’s loss was, really, quite
A commonplace affair.
Alas ! four gentlemen of Perth
Went sailing in a yacht:
The wind, despite their sterling worth,
The better of them got ;
Yea, like the Martha’s crow they died,
And so, witli one consent,
The grieving daily journals cried—
“A shocking accident!”
Bear this in mind, dear wealthy friends ;
Observe, ye poor “ sea-dogs ’’ —
The shock of sudden death depends
Entirely on its togs ;
It overtakes well-tailored men,
And horror fills the air.
We deem the doom of Sailor Ben
A commonplace affair.

Ed. Bulletin, — To correct a widely-held
wrong impression gathered from your time to
time press reference to Mr. J. H. Geddes’ exploits
in England and the Continent in connection with
the N.S. W. meat and produce- trade, will you, in
justice to the Pastoral Finance Association
Limited (of which I am a member), allow me to
state that all the heavy expenses of Mr. Geddes’
mission were paid by the Association named. In
failing to at any time make reference to the
principals in this laudable undertaking you not
only neglect to “give honor where honor is due,”
but allow the idea to go unchecked, that Mr.
Geddes in this matter acted as an agent for
Geddes, Birt and Co. Ld., and not for the Pas-
toral Finance Association Ld. To prevent any
possible mistakes, let me say that the present
Pastoral Finance Association Ld. (formerly trading
under the title of J. H. Geddes and Co.) is a Co.
quite distinct from the present Geddes, Birt and
Co. Ld.—Yours faithfully, Thos. Hungereoed.

No official openly accused of causing the death of
a patient by drunken neglect should get “ another
chance ” without establishing his innocence at a
public investigation. The only proper course for
the medical officers, in face of the deliberate insult
conveyed by the committee’s decision to hush up
this hideous scandal, is to resign. This Narandera
hospital affair is the most disgraceful and squalid
scandal which has reached The Bulletin’s ears
for a long while. Unfortunately, however, it is
by no means unique. Within the past 12 months
similar scandals have arisen with regard toother
N.S.W. country hospitals, where unfortunate
patients appear to have died under circumstances
the contemplation of which makes one’s blood run
cold. And in all these cases the ugly business
has been “ hushed up.”

A high New Australia official is said to
have refused medical assistance to some of his
settlers’ wives who were about to become mothers,
on the ground that “natural” mothers ought not
to need a doctor. What difference is there
between the mental condition of such a man and
that of the Sydney clergyman who objected to his
wife wearing stays because “ the mother of Jesus
never wore stays ? ”

naice”! Realising recently that the imported

At Toowoomba, Miss Black won first-prize in the Ladies' rifle competition against

As The Bulletin predicted, the additional
responsibility of the sale of tickets has made
Sydney tram-conductors’ lives barely endurable.
Seventy-five per cent, of people never bother to
buy a ticket till they get into a tram ; and one
man, the other day, actually offered a for
sixpeuu’orth of tickets, and demanded change.
* * *
A current Sydney story says that the body
of an infant was lately found at the gate of a
suburban house, and a verdict of wilful murder
was returned against some person unknown.
There was absolutely nothing to connect any
inmate of the house with the matter; but it is
alleged that an over-zealous constable, learning
that a young lady visitor had recently come down
from the country, jumped to a hasty conclusion
and compelled her to undergo an examination by
the Govt, medical officer, which resulted in her
complete exoneration. It is stated there was no
evidence to justify even the slightest suspicion, and
the shame and annoyance of the lady may be
imagined. If the facts are as stated, they are
worse than disgraceful to the police administra-
tion, and the public are entitled to demand prompt
inquiry and redress.

to music. ”

A Q. daily paper advt. :
"Yy ANTED, Dentist’s “ Show-pieces.” Apply etc.
It is an easy matter to follow those “show-pieces.”
In a week or two they will appear, amidst much
gold paint, in a main street doorway, with the
intimation that Mr. Abraham Bedoodlestein,
D.D.S., late of London and New York, has
commenced operations—“full sets from £l.”
Then Mrs. Mumbles, having decided that
she “ must see about them teeth,” will
take Mr. Mumbles in tow and start out
in search of something cheap. The beautiful
sets in Bedoodlestein’s show-cases settle them ; in
they go, and Mrs, Mumbles has her model taken
(on impression-wax which does duty for all
Bedoodlestein’s “ patients ” —he bought only half-
a-pound to start with), pays a deposit, and waits
anxiously for the time when she can attack a
well-trained bullock. The week following Mrs.
Mumbles calls again, and Bedoodlestein puts into
her mouth a combination of vulcanite and porce-
lain, which feels very uncomfortable, but which
he assures her will be all right “ when the suction
takes.” She waits some time, in much pain and
discomfort, for “the suction to take,” which never
eventuates. After Bedoodlestein has vainly en-
deavored to make the set fit, by warming them
■over a spirit-lamp, and attempting to force them
to the shape of her palate, he says her gums have
shrunk since he took the impression, and wants
15s. to re-model the set. That is all the satisfac-
tion she can get out of him, and Mr. Mumbles,
who is not so keen a searcher after the economic
as his wife is, takes her to a respectable tooth-
artist, who makes Mrs. Mumbles feel ill by point-
ing out to her that the “ set ” is made up of old
teeth, taken from discarded sets which have seen
much service in the mouths of other of Badoodle-
stein’s “ patients.”

Spaguoletti : “ Circumstances have domi-
ciled me in the vicinage of married Chinese half-
castes. The woman is a desperate toiler. She
chops the wood, harnesses the horse up in the
dray, takes him out, stables and feeds him and
hauls off the loading, while the husband does
nothing. Hardly Australian, or even English, to
take a lower stand.”
• # *
Poor busted Melbourne has a new worry—•
the Bible-fiend. When he knocks at the front-
door the occupant sees a clerical-looking individual
with a black-bag who at once asks, “ Can I speak
with you for a few minutes about the Scripture? ”
She, thinking it a new parson, asks him to step in
and sit down. He does so, opens his bag, and
presently the poor woman finds that she is enter-
taining a canvasser unawares. And, as everybody
knows, Scriptural touts are harder to choke off
than the racecourse article.

Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will he taken of communications insuffi .
ciently stamped. Name and address must
appear on MS. No liability is undertaken re
contributions voluntarily submitted, and in no

Geo. Lash Gordon, who died so suddenly in Sydney last
week, was a man of some ability, but bitterly disappointed,
and another round peg in a square hole. He had no little
power as a dramatist, and missed the second or third rung
of the ladder. There is no reason to believe he was much
inferior to such as Brandon Thomas, v*ho hits with a
“Charley's Aunt.”
Melb. people wondered why the nearly-nude figures
were withdrawn from the Princess programme on the
■ occasion of Gov. Hopetoun's farewell visit. As a matter
■of fact. His Ex. wote requesting that the show should
mot be given, so a one-act curtain-raiser was s ibstiluted.
Melba, it is said, won’t allow her promised visit to Aus-
tralia to interfere with her season at Covent Garden
Theatre.
Arthur Garner told Melb. Insolvency Court, the other
.day, that his third share of profits during the last five
years of .the Triumvirate Firm’s existence averaged
£4500 per annum. The Firm’s best year was one of their
■first. As the result of those twelve blissful months they
cutup £30,000 between them, and started to stuff the
■public with theatrical delicacies. The Firth has been
plucky, in a somewhat one-horse country.

Nearly every Melb. suburb now has its juvenile minstrel
00. who “tour” the environs in vans. You often see
vans driven along the streets, each containing upwards of
20 “ performers ” of from 4 to 16 years old, and of assorted
sexes. Outside the van is a calico sign stating that it is
such-and-such a juvenile minstrel co. The juveniles in
one of these troupes, which “ shows ” at a hall in Bourke-
street every Saturday night, are described as “stars, the
best in the city." The admission is “by silver coin,”
and it is added—“ Carriages at 10.30 ” I

IT IS EASY TO ASK QUESTIONS.
A child can ask questions that a wise man can’t
answer. Yes ; and there are some questions that
the ablest doctors don’t like to have people ask
ithem. Say a question of this sort —while we are
about it, however, we might as well tell the story
straight away and have done with it.
Mrs. Sarah Mace was very ill with influenza.
■That was in February, 1892, the time of the
■epidemic. The attack was severe. She was
■suddenly seized with a violent pain in the head
((both front and back) and temples. she had pain
in all her joints, too, and was hot and feverish.
She went to bed and sent for the doctor. He came,
examined her, took her temperature—over 100
degrees—and said very little, like a wise man.
The lady could not turn herself in bed. She lived
on slops, such as milk and broth ; she could not
swallow a morsel of solid food. Her husband
pressed hsr to take strong food, saying, “If you
don’t eat you will starve.” Right enough ; but he
forgot that when one can’t eat he can’t eat, and
there’s an end.
Well, she got weaker and weaker, and fell away
until there wasn’t much left of her but skin and
bone. Then, in her anxiety, she put her question ;
“ Doctor, do you think I shall get well ?"
The doctor’s answer was true and honest; we
like and respect him for it. He said, “ Mrs. Mace,
Ido not know." He couldn’t tell. Neither could
any other doctor. No ; not even if he had a string
of titles to his name as long as a kite’s tail. But
did she get well? Wait a bit. We must hark
back a minute now.
Along about the 10th of March, 1880, Mrs. Mace
(began to feel tired, languid and weary, as if her
■work was too much for her, she said. She had a
ibad taste in the mouth, poor appetite, and what-
ever she ate gave her pain at the chest and heart.
-“I had,” she continues, “a dull heavy pain at the
■right side and between the shoulders, and a sinking
•■feeling at the pit of the stomach, and a rising of
ifoul gas in my throat. Later on I had rheumatic
ipains in my heart, chest and back. I sent for a
■doctor, who attended me for six weeks, but I got
worse. One day the cook said, ‘ Why don’t you
try Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup?’—this being
a medicine my mistress (I was living with a lady
in Camden Town) kept in the house for family use.
I did so, and was soon as strong as ever.
Years fled away, and in 1891 she had the first
attack of influenza, and in 1892, the second, al-
ready described in part. We now complete the
account of the 1892 attack.
Mrs. Mace says: “The doctor continued to
attend me for five weeks, and I took his medicines
for that time, but gained no strength. After hav-
ing suffered for seven weeks I said to my husband,
* I will now see what Mother Seigel’s Curative
Syrup will do for me. Perhaps it may cure
influenza as well as other ailments.’ I carried out
this resolution ; began taking the Syrup, and was
soon on my feet again, and have since kept in
excellent health, taking an occasional dose when
needed. (Signed) Sakah Mace, Monk’s Farm,
Great Warley, Brentwood, Essex, August 27th,
1892.” •
Now we have never advertised Mother Seigel’s
■Curative Syrup as a remedy for influenza. Yet
the facts stated by Mrs. Mace cannot be disputed.
What is the conclusion ? A very simple one in-
deed. She did not have influenza until her system
Iliad lietui debilitated by indigestion and dyspepsia.
It is such people generally (almost wholly) that
all epidemic diseases attack. The dreaded cholera
scarcely ever touches anybody except a dyspeptic
—cholera is a malady of the bowels only. The
•more we hear of disease the more the proof piles
mp, showing that persons with a sound digestion
■art safe against dying of anything except violence
or old age. The gyrup cured Mrs. Maca’s influenza
by taking away the ground it stood on —the tor-
pidity of her digestion.
She once asked, “ Doctor, do you think I shall
get well?” Time and Mother Seigel have said,
“Yes.”

A well-known actress in Vienna is said to have been
dismissed from the company because she wouldn’t allow
her stage-lovers, or even her stage-fathers, to kiss her.
Reason : she had only lately been married, and her real
husband was jealous. Poor devil ! He goes to the
theatre every night and watches his wife carefully.
Sooner or later something will happen to take him off
guard, and the belief that his w ife was devoured with
kisses on that particular evening will grow on him until
he goes mad, or takes to drink. They generally take to
drink and whack their idols.
Dear Bulletin, —John Lemmone’s London success is
not surprising. Instrumentalists who have heard the best
of the European flautists, and those who have heard Rad-
clifle, Schadb, and other cracks in Australia, have, ail
along, been unanimously of opinion that Lemmone could
hold his own anywhere. Radcliffe produces some start-
ling effects, and his execution is phenomenal; but the
Australian has a certain native delicacy of style, combined
with fine execution, in which he is unrivalled. As a com-
poser he will also take high rank. His “ Polka Caprice ”
is unique, and his more ambitious compositions should
acquire a wide popularity. Strange as it may appear,
envy plays no part in the feelings which Australian
flautists bear towards “Jack” Lemmone. Never was
there a more modest or popular star, and all flute-land
rejoices to hear of his success—a success all the more
deserved when is taken into consideration the comparative
lack of opportunity which instrumentalists, away from
the great musical centres, have of perfecting themselves.
With Lemmone out of the way, there should be a good
opening in Australia for amateur Moss, of Dunedin (M.L.).
He is quite young, still improving, and is already a very
superior performer—at his best in the classics of Kuhlau.
But Moss is of the Chosen ; and, preferring the certain
emoluments of a good commercial traveller’s situation to
the uncertainty of artistic life, has refused several tempt-
ing offers.— Flauto Magico.
Miss Marie Elster’s benefit, coming off at Melb. Theatre
Royal 9th April, promises to be a real success. All the
magnates (social, judicial, parliamentary and otherwise)
are disposed to recognise the merits of the good-looking
songstress now that she is going away. “Ohoufleuri”
complete, garden-scene from “Faust,” and first act of
“ Maritana,” are the chief items of the programme. Miss
Elster’s place is on the stage, but she is going first under
Marohesi —as this seems to be the required thing since
Melba’s success. Sir W. Robinson has written that
he has letters of introduction ready for her, and that he
is going to compose her a. song himself when the right
mood comes on. Miss Elster has done her quota for
charily, and for this she ought to be rememb red in her
own hour of want of funds. Miss Elster’s stage-name is
taken, like Mde. Melba’s, from the place of her residence.
When she first fnade singing her profession she lived at
Elsternwick.
“Bob” Cunningham, the Melb. tenor, now on his way
back from London with a concert co., achieved a great
success in the Big Smoke, where he sang on the same
platform with Santley, Foli, Ben Davies, Sterling and
Alice Gomez.
Dear Bulletin,— Grundy, in writing “ A Bunch of
Violets,” recently produced at Melb. Bijou, must ha\e
been greatly influenced by Mrs. Grundy. He is at no
little pains to caricature offensively the working man in
politics. Few dramatists will allow the working man to
meddle in politics without making an ass of him, and
Grundy makes him more of an ass than usual, and a good
deal of the hog into the bargain. A deputation of work-
ing men introduced is too grossly ridiculous, even for
Tory-supported burlesque. Every man of the party is a
bottle-nosed, frowsy, crapulous dead-beat, who bellows
insane appl iuse at the most transparent bunkum and
palpable “borack” spoken by the pious candidate. Their
leader is an unconscionable German, a gaol-bird and a
disgusting sneak, who sells the working men’s vote in the
lump. Grundy allow'S some good points to his rascally
Baronet, hypocrite, embezzler, bigamist, and thief, but
does not condescend to relieve with even a faint suggestion
of common-sense the gluttony, drunkenness and dirt of
the working man who presumes to be political. One quiet,
sensible toiler, who had washed himself, and who was
perfectly sober, and had decent patches on his pants, and
wasn’t prepared to sell his soul for 18d., might have im-
proved the odour of “The Bunch of Violets ” vastly, but
Grundy couldn’t place him. Strange that the British
workman, who is so well washed and combed whilst he
confines himself to the temperance movement and Bible-
classes, should run to rags, and beer, and dirt, and im-
morality immediately he touches politics.—E.D.
