one of them was “shouted” a seat by the other and killed
him in consequence.
Melbourne hoisted its umbrellas the other day on hear-
ing the rumbling of thunder. But it was only Charlie
Holloway conversing with Edwin Kelly in Bourke-streel.
Rev. H. R. Haweis, Smylhe’s new star, pronounces his
name Hoarse—a nice name for a lecturer.
One gleam from “The Masqueraders”: “ I believe that
bad people when they die go to Saturn, where they are
set to study the current theology for 500 years. If at the
end of that time they know anything about it, their pro-
bation is complete.” It doesn’t look much in print; but
a very small thing in print goes a very long way on the
stage—yea, or on the judicial bench. Just listen to His
Honor’s puns!
SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Vio.-English cricket-match was an anti-
climax in which neither players nor public took much
interest. Stoddart stood out on account of his cold, and
the Englishmen’s first innings only readied 131. Vic.
followed with 269; England made 270; and Vi a got the
133 required to win with 7 wickets and 3 runs to spare.
♦ * * •
In the match England v. Victoria the manage-
ment pouched shekels they would otherwise have lost hy
admitting spectators to the ground for 6d. after 3.30. The
innovation is a good one.
* * • ♦
The Englishmen’s performance against Victoria
was discreditable, but quite natural under the circum-
stances. They batted anyhow, and fi Ided, in many
instances, as though the few’ people present hadn t paid
for a serious show.
• • » »
The speeches of the amateur rnemoers of Stod-
dart’s team at the farewell Melb. Club banquet were per-
fect models of the dude style of talk, doncherknow.
Stoddart informed the company six distinct thin s that he
had “an awfully jolly trip.” Wicket-keeper Phillipson
said lie was “devilish glad” he had come, and the long
Ford said he would have been “deucedly sowy ” if he
hadn’t, whilst Lancashire Maclaren remarked that he
would be “awfully delighted” to come again. Briggs
merely stated that, he had had a jolly good time, and
wanted another trip of the same kind.
• • * •
The Melb. Cricket-club goes out of its way to
perpetuate and accentuate the snobbish English practice
of distinguishing between “amateurs” and “profes-
sionals.” At all the luncheons the Misters have occupied
the top table and the pro.’s have been stowed among the
crowd. This was the case at the farewell banquet, but
the assembled eaters showed their contempt for this pro-
ceeding by yelling for Maclaren. Ford and Co. exactly as
they shouted for plain Johnny Briggs. Another odious
feature of the M.C.C.’s snobbishness in this direction was
the giving of honorary life-membership medals to the
“gentleman players” only.
• « « •
Judge A’Beckett, at the Stoddart banquet, con-
fessed, amidst much merriment, that during the final
test-match the Supreme Court Judges got frequent
bulletins of the game gravely handed up to them by
counsel in the guise of “ notes in the case.”
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —Joy is but the foretaste of
sorrow. Pleasure is the sugar coating on the pill of
despair. Surely, too surely, i predicted disaster for Aus-
tralia as a necessary consequence of the cable-stoppage
that for eight mad days protected us from European
news. The cable-liar was gagged as no Gould could gag
him. For a joyous week London Times did not approve
the opinion of the Argus or the S.M. Herald with regard
to frozen meat. For a week the death of no second sons
of second-rate nonentities was wired to Australia.
Every omen said disaster. The disaster came in the fifth
test-match. For me, I forestalled my fate. I had dis-
ciplined my soul into calm with the pantomimes—while
the continent mourned I was at peace. I had conquered
the worst. —Y.Z.
• • * *
Blackliam greatly admires Johns, the Melb.
University stumper, who kept wickets against England
last week, and regards him as ills legitimate successor
in the Vic. team, if not in Australia. The pad-and-
glove men are cropping up in every province. Vic. lias
Blackliam, Johns and Lewis; N.S.W, Kelly; and S.A.,
Clem. Hill and Jarvis, all first-rate men.
♦ * » *
There is not much doubt now that an Australian
team will leave for Hingland in a year’s time. The popu-
lar “ Jack ” Portua w-as suggested as manager, but his
recent Govt, appointment will perhaps forbid. Phil. Sheri-
dan may undertake the job. Unless a great reversal of
form takes place next season, five new men, at least, will
be in the team, viz., Eady (Tas.), Albert Trolt (Vic.), Ire-
dale (N.S.W.), and Darling and Clem. Hill. Young Hill
will be taken as second stumper.
• * * *
Of the players representing Australia now,
Giffen and B ackhiam are the only ones who were in the
memorable match in Aug., ’B2, when Australia beat Eng-
land by 7 runs.
* * * *
Jack Lyons, playing in a club-match, March 16,
made 70 out of 80 runs scored on a bad wicket w-hile he
was in. The ball went oat of the ground twice, and five
times over the chains. On the same day Jones, the
“ Adelaide Express,” bowled 8 wickets for 23 runs.
» ♦ ♦ ♦
A popular curate is stationed in a township in
the Christchurch (M.L.) region. After service on Sunday,
for th -b nefit of the small boys of his congregation, he
reads out in church the latest football or cricket items.
A few years ago he was a clever 13-stone forward of a
crack team. He is much liked by ail creeds and classes,
and if the youthful portion of his flock could manage it
he would be immediately created a Church, of England
Bishop—in fact, the English Primacy itself would be at
once his but for the fact that acceptance would involve
his removal from the district.
* * * *
English papers unanimously declare Giffen to be
the greatest cricketer in the world, which de-pedestalises
the mighty Grace.
* * * *
The “Continentals” given at,Adelaide Exhi-
bition for George Giffen’s honor and benefit yielded £45
clear of expenses.

In 99 cases out of 100 the better the bowler the
worse the captain.
» ♦ # »
Ward and Richardson ran 150 yards on
the M.O.C. Ground, last Friday morning, for a small
wager. Ward beat the express bowler in a common
canter.
• • ♦ *
There will apparently be no Vic. football-boom
this year, as there is no money in hand to feed the
hungry player with. The Melb. Club has kept about a
dozen dodging round its ground through the cricket
season, and has found it uncomfortably expensive.
• * » »
The V.R.C. must soon follow the example of
the A.J.0., and give monthly meetings.
♦ W ♦ ♦ ♦
If in anything like his old form, Bungebah (Bst.
131 b.) is fairly “chucked into” the Doncaster. He is
getting old in years, but then geldings last, and, above
all, the Grandmaster’s do.
* ♦ ♦ •
For his first English season at least, Carbine is
to be reserved for the Duke of Portland’s own mares. He
is reckoned a cheap purchase by the knowing ones up
top, who compare ids price with the £25,000 paid for the
roaring Ormonde, and the £20,000 paid for St. Blaise. It
is estimated that when “Old Jack f ’ is open to breeders
lie will soon get back the £13,650 which he cost, as a run
on him is anticipated.
* * • *
Net a single favorite got home at the Hopetoun
complimentary meeting.
‘ * * * *
The Hopetoun Farewell meeting at Flemington
was another awful experici-e for punters. When the
average starting price of winners is 9 to X the unf >r-
tunates who back a loser every lime are in an overwhelm-
ing majority, under which circumstance the fact that an
earl happens to lie making his last appearance on the
scene of desolation doesn't count for much. As a special
occasion for horsey grovel, last Saturday’s meeting proved
a failure. Not more than fifty loyalists, old V.U.O,
identities for the most part, demonstrated over Gov.
Hopetoun’s departure after the fifth race had fallen
to n rank outsider, and of these not more than
ten joined in the “one cheer more for the Countess."
Her ladyship certainly didn’t poll a dozen shouts. A few
female leaders of sassiety came down to ttie rails beside
the judge’s box, with the avowed hope of hearing a greet
ovation, or something, but the ovation or something never
came to pass. The people who had been expected to roll
up and make a noise in Hopetoun’s honor were going
their respective ways, cursing their blanky luck.
Lady Hopetoun, sporting the familiar cheeked
gown, with magenta fixings* which lias served tier many
tini 'S and oft. at the races, made a farewell tour of
Fh niington birdcage while the steeplechasers were being
saddled. On the subject of hors s she can be almost
eloq lent, and in birdcage society ttie fair descendant of
Mullins gets a'.ong comfortably. Bnsaco was the
pnticular animal that Lady Hopetoun had come
f rib for to see, and old Alec. Tav lor, of
lit I Inrat, was just girthing the favourite when she
a rived. Rather an alf ding spectacle, that parting
interview h tween Ali o and Ins nohl ■ friend. She
held the old ’mi’s band (of which sporting journalists fre--
qnentiy remark that it “has not yet. lest its cunning ’’) as
though loth to leave him, and perhaps there was a tear in
tier Irish eye when she turned to go. Evidently Alec and
the Countess had met in ttie Ballarat district, and one
touch of horse had made them kin.
♦ ♦ * ♦
“Jack” Brewer, who had his wrist broken
when Busaco fell in the Holywell Steeplechase, is now
probable the best rider over the “ small sticks ’’ in Aus-
tralia.
• • * *
Tiie S. A. J.C. have concluded to buy Morphet-
ville racecourse, near Adelaide, for £12;000 :an illustration
of the benefit of the totalizator. The two big S.A.
racing-clubs are the wealthiest in Australia. ■ A few- years
ago Morphetville racecourse was sold, lock, stock and
barrel, by mortgagees, and the two clubs were practically
defunct.
• * * •
The Bulletin has for years been as a voice cry-
ing in the Australian wilderness for the ini reduction of
the totalisator ; now, even the proprietary clubs are hol-
lering for it with all the energy of men who know that
their living is in danger.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
Ingomar, a Maoriland stallion, which, a year or
so back, the cognoscenti would not stand at any price, is
coming to the front. Though he has had few 7 chances
with good mares, he has sired Barbarian, Bessie Macarthy
and Mahaki.
» * * *
Melb. Herald publishes a suggestion that, in
steeplechases, the ground on the landing-side of the
obstacles should be ploughed.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
The starting-machine is like the camera, inas-
much as although it can’t lie it can go very near it.
Twice at successive meetings at Caulfield, and each time
in the last race, a contretemps has occurred, and the .
machine has refused to act.
* * * •
Tommy Hales rode his first Melb. Cup in ’72 —
second on The Ace. Tommy has done well since then,
but the mao who beat him (W. Enderson) is a Melb. sales-
yardsman and by no means a millionaire. Yet in the
Seventies he was the best jock, in all Australia-flat,
hurdle, or steeple.
• * * •
An old-time jockey who carried most popuiat
colors to Melb. Oup victory early in the ’7o’s is now brick-
laying in Melbourne.

It would be interesting to trace the history .of ,
the different jockeys who, during the past 30 years, have
steered Oup-winners, and for the time were worshippid.
None was ever known to retire with a fortune, but a good
many have been known to retire without one.

* • * *
Champion mean man has been discovered at
Masterton (M.L.). After selling a horse he wanted to
take his shoes off, insisting that they weren’t in the
bargain.
• * ♦ ♦
The sudden death of John L. Sullivan from
heart-disease emphasises the generally-true axiom that
big muscles mean a weak constitution. Searle died as
suddenly ; and a similar end is prophesied for Sandow,
despite his careful living. But probably whisky had most
right to drive Sullivan’s hearse. Sinew lasts; muscle
doesn’t.
« • * •
The late Bill Branch laid 903 to 9 against the
Pearl winning the Melb. Oup of’7l. The man who won
it is working now for Melb. Corporation. That is about
the longest Cup odds to date.
• • * •
Sep. Miller (new Y.R.C. chairman) is a true son
of Money Miller. His neddies must earn their oats and
pick up all the crumbs in and round Melb. small meet-
ings. His St. Leger win was his first classical flat
success. He never backs his horses or pays a fancy price
f->»* fhem. The late chairman, C. B. Fisher, was just the
opposite.
Robinson Crusoe, one of the big prizes in the
St. Albans lottery, is 22 years old. He won the ’76 A. J.O.
Derby. He still bears the marks of his sensational si a-
« nvnerienoes, and he can still get winners with the
best of them.

Thus a Charters Towers man returned
from New Australia : “There was a lot of grum-
bling about the Dibbs Government making the
Royal Tar double its stock of provisions, but after
seven weeks’ voyage we were told to be careful
or we should have to go on short rations. . .
We sent to Villa Rica for milch goats, and Lane
sent 13, of which 10 were billies. . . The Para-
guayans thought we were ‘ muy rioa ’ (very rich)
because we wore clothes. . . You may get 20
men to live together as Socialists, but you’ll never
get 20 women.” Here follows a lively account of
a feminine revolution excited by a distribution of
three straw hats. In fact, all through the narra-
tive, the enraged female is very much to the front
—on one occasion with an axe in her fist.

CLEMENTS TONIC
RESTORES VIGOUR, HEALTH
AND VITALITY OF YOUTH.
CUBES
‘ WEAKNESS,
LASSITUDE,

PROMPTLY AND PERMANENTLY.
BEWARE OF IMITATIONS.

ESPECIALLY THIS ONE.
Mr. William Baker, Brush, Oooranbong, N.S. W.,
who writes on May 27th, 1893 : —As one of those
who have received great benefit from Clements
Tonic, I feel grateful and in duty bound to con-
vey to you in writing my gratitude for blessings
received. I have been troubled for some time
with violent coughing and spitting first thing in
the morning, back-aching, tired and aching limbs
all over, head-ache and dizziness so that I would
reel and fall if I did not happen to be near some-
thing to hold on to with my hands. I tried many
things, but found very little relief until one day

How people may disappear in the bush.
In the ’9O flood a lot of blacks were camped near
Gunyan, on the Dumaresq, and one, old Nanny,
aged about 80 or 90, was swept away. In ’95 the
remains of Nanny were found not very far off in
a heap of flood debris which had been set on fire.
It was quite close to a racecourse, where races
have been held every year since, yet old Nanny
quietly reposed th re all the time. Since then the
bones have been distributed as curios.
Whoever reverences things that are old,
including ruins, and mummies, and the legends ■ f
the Rhine, and the Church, and the institution ■ f
monarchy, should use Elixir dentifrice, for width
Metayer and Co., of 8 O’Connell-street, Sydney,
are wholesale agents. It was invented by a holy
Benedictine father, of the French Abbey of Sonlac,
just 522 years ago, and has commanded a good sale
ever since. It was the first dentifrice used in the
dark and dirty middle ages, and its long continuance
may be set down as reliable proof of its excellence.
Also, the Benedictine fathers had a fine taste in
scents and perfumes, and all manner of luxuries,
and what they did for their own comfort was
always done well. Therefore, it may be safely
assumed that the old father of 1373 turned out a
valuable article.
Victoria's most persistent litigant, now
that Silas Hard ng is no more, is a man who three
years ago had a small grievance about a culvert.
He sued the shire council over it three times in
various courts, and at last got a verdict with costs.
Then he was found going round with a gun after
seme of the witnesses on the other side, and was
committed for trial by the local injustices. The
Crown didn’t proceed with the case, and the
aggravated plaintiff next applied for and obtained
an order with costs to quash the decision of the
local court in committing him for trial. Then the
Crown got another Judge to quash the order to
quash the decision of the local court with costs.
After that, plaintiff applied to the Full Court to
quash this last order, but his appeal was dis-
missed witli costs, so lie will now probably take
his famous case to the I’rivy Council. Which all
goes to show how six or eight lawyers can get a
g i >d living for three years out of a very ordinary
culvert.
It has long been recognised that tobacco is
an invaluable disinfectant and purifier, as well as
an unfailing agent in the destruction of all para-
sitic insect life. From this proposition depends
the great reputation of the Excelsior Nicotine
Sheep-dip, whose chief constituents are nicotine
and carbolic, and Mr. Blackett, the Vic. Govt,
analyst, says : The value of the tobacco-dip
depends on the quantity of nicotine, its solubility
in water, and on its retaining its principles un-
changed in solution. The Excelsior Nicotine Dip
possesses all three qualities, and is recommended
to all sheep-owners as an effective preventative
and cure of scab, ticks, lice, acari, &c. Messrs.
Weber, Lohmann, and Co., of Melb. and Sydney,
arc sole Au tralian agents.

Fac-simile of Cheque for £2500, received for part of Victorian
rights in No, 1 Patent Investment—First Dividend on Original
Subscription of £5OO.
Profits on Patents. —“ More money has been, and always oan be, made out of patented in-
ventions than by any other investments.”
Essentials to Success ■with Patents. —(1.) A good and novel invention. (2.) Capital to
protect and develop the invention THROUGHOUT THE WORLD. (3.) Experience in appraising
the intrinsic merits of an invention, its prospects of success, and, after securing protection, in deve-
loping and introducing it on the world’s markets—in short, Business Management.
Good inventions on the one hand, capital on the other, are comparatively plentiful. THE AUS-
TRALIAN PATENTS DEVELOPMENT COMPANY is projected to provide the third equally
important essential, business management.
The success attending the privately conducted PATENT INVESTMENTS Nos. 1 and 2, and
the numerous flattering applications from inventors and investors alike, convince the management
that there is room for the extension of its operations over a much wider field than was at first contem-
plated.
The Company’s modus operandi is as follows: Selecting the first essential, viz., “a good and
novel invention,” from amongst the many offered for approval, the Company invites subscriptions
to a development fund—in this case of .£lOOO in 1000 shares of £1 each. This provides the second
essentia], “ Capital to develop and protect the invention.”
The business relations established throughout the world in dealing with their other valuable
patents, and the experience of the Company’s officers and experts afford the third essential, “ Business
Management.” ' •
The Management can confidently recommend their No. 3 Investment to subscribers as a thoroughly
safe speculation, and one certain to bring in very handsome returns. It is anticipated that the sales
of patent rights and licenses to manufacture the invention selected (which is an article of everyday use
in every household) will produce a nett return of from one to three million pounds sterling, and that
it will commence to pay handsomely immediately the patents are fully secured, giving a dividend
exceeding the original subscription within six months.
The invention is perfected, and patents are being secured in every part of the world. The funds
subscribed are used exclusively for this purpose. No part of the fund goes to inventor until nett
profits are declared, of which he takes one-third.

APRIL, 1895.
TICKETS AT SPECIALLY LOW
will be issued at Sydney and Suburban Stations
to the Country by Special Trains leaving Sydney,
as under :
TO THE NORTHERN LINE, Bth APRIL.
Fares between Sydney and other stations
arranged at proportionately low rates.
These tickets will only be available on the out-
ward journey by the above special trains (Culcairn
and Oorowa line excepted), but passengers can
return by the ordinary trains (Melbourne express
excepted) on any date up to and including TUES-
DAY, 23rd April.
Passengers for stations on the Culcairn-Corowa
line will leave Sydney by the 5.15 pum. express on
MONDAY, Bth April, and change at Culcairn.
RETURN TICKETS AT SPECIALLY LOW
FARES will also be issued tb SYDNEY from all
stations by train leaving Jennings 5.35 a.m., and
Albury 2.45 p.tn., Bourke 8 a.m. on TUESDAY,
9th April; Nowra at 2.30 p.m., and Shellharbour
at 2.45 p.m. on WEDNESDAY, 10th April,
RETURN TICKETS at TWOPENCE per
mile first-class (minimum 10s.) and ONE PENNY
per mile second-class (minimum 55.) will also be
issued from stations on the Southern Line and
Branches to stations between Mittagong and
Goulburn inclusive, and from stations on the
Western line and branches to stations between
Bowenfelsand Emu Plains inclusive, by the above
trains.
These tickets will only be available on the out-
ward journey by the trains named, but passengers
can return from Sydney by ordinary trains (Mel-
bourne Express excepted) on any date up to and
including WEDNESDAY, 24th April.
PASSENGERS from the Corowa Branch Line
can return from Sydney by the 5.15 p.m. Express
on Mondays, Wednesdays, and Fridays up to
WEDNESDAY, 24th April.
Further particulars can be obtained from all
station-masters, and at the Central Booking Office,
George-street, Sydney.
By order of the Commissioners.
H. M'LACHLAN, Secretary.

EXTRACTS
FROM
PRIVATE LETTERS.
“It is with heartfelt grati-
tude I write to tell you of the
great benefit my little daugh-
ter Gladys has derived from
‘Benger's Food.' The doctor
and all who saw her thought
we should never he able to
rear her. We took her to the
hospital, and the authori-
ties recommended ‘ Beuger's
Food.’ From that time she
began to pick up flesh and
strength.”
“If every mother knew of
Its value no other would bo
used.”
Tailor-made suits of all-wool Maoriland
tweed are advertised in Auckland at 355.
Dear Bulletin, —An Australian country
hospital is a queer place. A while ago I was
trained nurse in one, which was favoured with two
physicians —Dr. X. and Dr. Z. Dr. X. was
seized with a mania for experimenting. He
grappled with the “ ” life-elixir
theory, and decided to test the vile fluid on his
own account in the benevolent ward. He took
the superintendent into his confidence and in-
structed him to tell me the injections consisted
of morphia. The sup. disobeyed his orders
and told me the truth, so I was able to
make private notes of all the cases. Dr. X.
had three beds in the benevolent ward. ( Dr.
Z. had one. Dr. X., who, as usual, wasn’t on
speaking terms with his colleague, had paved
the way to success by assuring his three
old geysers—whose ages ranged from 74 to 89
—that in a week’s time they would feel
twenty years younger. He went through all
sorts of manoeuvres to get me out of the ward
while he raved to them about the new treatment.
His precautions were wasted, as the senile and
loquacious patients invariably babbled to me all
about the cure which was to work sueh miracles.
The first patient to be treated was “Old Ned.”
He was an asthmaticky-rheumaticky subject, very
limp and helpless ; but he had his good days, and
he happened to have a good spell just at the time
of the first injection of the life-giving liquid.
This, combined with his faith in the new cure,
gave him unwonted vigor; he walked with an
almost straight back, and opened and shut his
gnarled fingers with marvellous rapidity. Dr. X.
was elated with his apparent success—so much so
that he told his friends, and by some means it
reached the ears of the local pressman, who
(doubtless, satirically) telegraphed the startling
news to the metropolitan dailies. Local excite-
ment ran high, and Dr. Z, asked meif I knew
anything of the matter. I told him all. To my
surprise, instead of joining me in my scepticism,
he seemed much impressed, and the next day said
he meant to try the thing for himself ! Dr. X.,
meanwhile, had treated the other two benevolents,
and they also improved hourly. Dr. Z.’s patient
felt neglected, and loudly demanded tobesupplied
with “ some o’ that ’ere stuff! ” Dr. Z. graciously
said: “So you shall be.” He took a little
bottle of whitish fluid out of his pocket, half-
filled a hypodermic syringe, and injected it
into the old man’s leg. Then he left the
ward, saying, “Now, you must not move
before daylight!” This seemed to me a queer
remark, but I guessed it had been made with
a purpose. This patient, known as “Mickey,”
was partially paralysed, and could not walk
without crutches—and then his toes hung straight
down from his heels and dragged on the floor. I
was on duty that night, staying up with a bad
fever case, and when I went into the old men’s
ward at dawn, the next day, I was just in time
to see “ Mickey ” walking from his bed to the
mantelpiece, to which he held very tightly and
surveyed my astonished face in triumph. “That
there stuff’s put the power back agin in my legs,”
he said ; and for two days it seemed the truth ;
but he collapsed on the third, and ever after was
lamer than ever. Two of Dr. X.’s rejuvenated
patients died from blood-poisoning, and the third
very nearly died. Dr. X. didn t advertise the
final result of his experiments, and the whole
thing was carefully hushed up. I left the hospital
soon after, and when I paid Dr. Z. a farewell
call, I showed him my notes of the Brown-Sequard
cases, “Scandalous,” he murmured, “as if the
subcutaneous injection of organic matter could
give an old person new life !” I said, quietly,
“You used milk-and-water, did you not?” He
replied, “You are not far wrong, nurse, but I
had an undoubted ‘result ’ for two days !’’ As I
remarked, an Australian country hospital is often

I he late C. B. Lowe’s (N.S.W.) book on horse-
breeding is at present hung up. When the poor fellow
died, writes Tub Bulletin’s London correspondent, he
left Allison, the “ Special Commissioner” of London
Sportsman, himself a recognised authority on breed-
ing, as his literary executor. Mr. H. C. White, with his
customary liberality, had given Lowe £3OO for the pur-
pose of bringing out the work, and this sum remains locked
up in a bank in London and will not be paid out until
Lowe’s estate is administered in England. By the
way, why has Mr. J. A. Scarr never published in book-
form liis clever articles on horse-breeding which appeared
i i old Bell’s Life in Sydney many years ago under the
signature of “ Puhdit ? ”
* * * *
Mr. W. C. Cooper, more or less of N.S.W.,
is not an absolute business genius, and was lately
hoist with his own petard. He owned the smart
colt Kirkeonnel, a second Derby favorite. Find-
ing, or thinking (and he may be wrong) that the horse
h el no chance of beating Raconteur, he determined to
sell him. He had insured him for a large sum, the
premium being £3OO a year, hut when he sold the horse
lie did not mention this fact! Had he done so the colt
would have- fetched considerably more than the 5000
guineas Blundell Maple gave for him. Cooper, however,
preferred to k -ep -.the insurance himself, intending to
claim in the event of the colt’s death. Maple heard of it,
and offered- £2OO for the policy, which had run for some
time, but Cooper wanted the whole premium he had
paid. Accordingly, he kept the policy—to discover when
too late that having parted with the animal ho had no
insurable interest, and that Lloyds would not pay if it
died,
♦ ♦ * *
The Y.R.C. is being asked to reduce the mini-
mum of stakes at suburban meetings to £2OO. Which
recalls a story about the veteran Bradshaw. “Old Brad”
had laid out a course (Richfield) near Mordialloc, which,
although within the suburban radius, was four miles from
the railway station ; consequently, £4OO stakes were out
of the question. But “ Braddy ” was equal to the occa-
sion. He put a race on the programme which he called
the Yellowhammer Stakes, value £2OO, open to the pro-
geny of Yellowhammer, As Yellowhammer had never
sireil more thart three quadrupeds in his life, and one was
dead, and the otlmr two in Brad’s own possession, a most
interesting race was looked for. They had both been
superannuated and had not been clipped for years. But
they were trott d out to race, and, after falling down a
couple of tim ‘s', managed to pass the winning-post, thus
saving “ Braddy ” the £2OO.

A certain good all-round Australian-born Syd-
ney sportsman, of old-time rowing and sailing celebrity,
now riiles a p>lo pony with a hanged tai l . In the old days,
he used to lie rioted for the courage of his opinions,
but time and the durned Britisher must havealtered him,
as the writer can scarcely believe that any Australian
lover of horseflesh can regard a banged tail as suitable for
wisping off Australian flies.
* * A * -
A Tasmanian tiglit-place vouched for by an old
fisherman. He was out off,shore, single-handed, “jig-
ging ” for barracouta. For a time he was very unsuc-
cessful-then fish came with a rush, fast and furious, and
he was lifting them out of the water as fast as he could
use the jig. At last the fish who were not hooked, noting
the upward flight of their brethren, thought there was •
something extra good “on the board” above water, and
they started “breaching’’—jumping into the boat from
all sides, chasing each other like a flock of sh ep,
while the Lone Fisherman chuckled with delight
at the grand freight he was getting so easily.
Presently, however, the matter became serious,
for the boat was getting far too deep for safety. Still
the fish came, and the captain and crew turned his atten-
tion to lightening cargo, and began throwing them out
as fast as he could, till the air was thick with fish going
and coming. He was utterly exhausted when the ’couta
ceased run dng, and as soon as he could pull himself
together he made for shore and safety. To this day he
declares that he never worked harder than when fighting
the plague of fishes to keep his boat afloat-and that he
will never in all his life again go jigging barracouta
single-handed. [The Bulletin how calls upon the Sydney
Society of Fish Liars to show its best card.]
* * * *
The late Duchess of Montrose was a daughter of
Lord Decies. His lovely sister married Hope, the mil-
lionaire novelist, an ugly man, author of “Anastasius.”
An artist painted the couple together. Hope wasn’t satis-
fied, and returned the picture. The artist changed his
head to a baboon, and exhibited the pair as Beauty and
the Beast. Denies cut the picture to ribbons. Artist
sued him. Case dismissed,
* ♦ ♦ *
Years ago very heavy play was indulged in on
the Australian coasting-steamers, and one captain (who
died not long ago) was noted for his luck. But he worked
on a system. Whenever a bad run set in,.his steward
somehow always came along with—“ The chief-officer
wants you on deck, sir,” and enabled him to retire with
at least some of his winnings. The signal was a clever
one, and was never spotted.
* * * »
Jobson had driven in his fast blood mare,
Molly, and was standing outside the Royal when the local
medico approaching asked Jobson his price for her. With
extreme gravity the latter replied, “Ten notes.” The
doctor, with much deliberation, felt her all over, thought
hard, and at length said, “ I’ll take her if I can sell one of
my own horses.” Jobson, unwilling to lose so good a
deal, persuaded the doctor to buy her, with the result
that the bargain was clinched on the spot, and those
assembled adjourned for drinks at the doctor’s expense.
As the transaction was a cash one, the doctor, after some
difficulty, due more to the stonebrokeness of his friends
than any other cause, borrowed the amount and handed
it over. “ What are these ?” said Jobson. “ Why, £XQ—
the sum you named,” replied the doctor. “Oh,” re-
marked Jobson with a quiet smile, “ my price was ten
notes—ten £5O notes.” Jobson and the doctor are not
cousins now.

A Vie. Hebrew swimmer lately competed at
some swimming matches at the opening of the new baths.
He was unsuccessful during the day, but carried off the
consolation race, the prize for which was a fine fat ham !
The irony of fate !

Haraar Bass, one of the bitter-beerists, paid
£80(Hor the fishing-rights of a Scottish salmon river, and
didn’t land a fish until two days before close time. Then

So the fish didn’t cost him much more than 40s. per lt>.

Mr. Taylor, one of the men who almost fatally
assaulted Arthur Cockburn, the late Abington’s pal, has
been captured in South Africa, and is only awaiting some
formality to be sent to England for trial. It is said that
Oockburn was offered £lOOO to withdraw from the case,
and refused it. Had Taylor settled Cockburn whilst he
was about it there would have been a public subscription
for his defence. Taylor is said to ho the most expert
card-player in the world. “ Pony " Moore is one of his
friends, and he it was who went hail for him.

length in a M.L, paper about his own past
iniquities. A few extracts
I, , hereby apologise for having slandered Messrs.
and , and all others, many in numbers. . . ,
That on several occasions I was guilty of searching the
pockets of coats belonging to some of the employes of that
firm with a view to stealing therefrom. . . . That
some years ago, on one, if not on more than one, occasion,
1 meanly stole some effervescing drink whilst the owners
of the shop were not present. . . . That about ten
years ago, when a hoarder and lodger at
, —, -, I stole half-a-orown (I
think this was the amount) from a vest pocket while
the owner lay asleep in bed. That on several occasions I
wilfully broke some of the by-laws of the city.
That I picked flowers in gardens (private) without asking
the owners’ permission to do so. That the expenses for
the supper and dance I gave at - a few years ago were
entirely defrayed from money out of which I had swindled
Messrs. and . That ray dealings when I sold fish
at, were not always strictly honest. I herewith beg
the pardon of all I have deceived and wronged in a,ny
way.
There is an itinerant evangelist in these parts at
present, and the dishonest fish-man has evidently
been “awakened.” By-and-bye, when the evan-
gelist is gone and the awakening is over, he will
be the maddest fish-man in Maoriland every time
he thinks of how he gave himself away. Whem a
man advertises that he is dishonest the public
always believes him enthusiastically. When he
further advertises that he isn’t going to be dis-
honest any more, the public only grins.

A Step Too High,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Te Parehao was a Maori chief, and Te
Parehao had something on his mind. He made no
sign, and worked away with spade and spear among
the gum-diggers as if his thoughts were as tranquil
as usual. Toward afternoon, though, when chance
threw him alone with Herewini, the oldest, and
therefore the most respected chief in Taiere, he
made known his trouble quietly, and asked
advice.
Herewini was grieved beyond measure when he
heard ; but he alone could not give counsel in a
case like this, he must consult the other rangatiras
of the settlement.
The meeting took place at dusk in Herewini’s
own whari. Five chiefs, all of high rank, were
present. They sat silently round a fire in the
middle of the floor, spitting into the coals. Te
Parehao, the sixth chief, and the one chiefly
interested, was late in arriving; but he came at
last and squatted quietly by the fire. Then
Herewini’s voice called on him to make known
the cause of this meeting; and the listeners moved
from the fire a little, and regarded Te Parehao
expectantly.
He introduced himself warrior fashion, then
reminded them that he had been husband four
months to a woman who was very pleasing to the
eye; that she only went away yesterday to see
her friends, who lived some two miles past the
pakeha’s house; and that it was a moonlight night
last night. Very well. He felt solitary, and
went out. He walked, he walked a long way,
and at last the home of the only white man in
Taiere was before his eyes. Then was he coming
back, but he saw through the trees a man and
woman going along to the house. Ah ! these
chiefs of high blood before him would feel sick
with shame, for the woman was her to whom he
had been married four months : the man was that
pu-rurry pakeha 1 Well, they went in, and he
waited and waited ; and the moon had gone
down, and it was near the rising of the sun when
the woman came out of the house and went back
to the home of her friends, two miles away. That
was all his korero, and he would ask the wise men
present what was to be done.
An old chief cleared his throat and spat impres-
sively into the fire. He had a few observations
to make. In the first place, the white man’s
punishment should be great, because he expected
too much. Not only did they allow him two
girls to keep house and wash, but he frequently
came and spent hours in the society of other
women, and wrong was not thought of it. And
now— a chief’s wife!—that was a step too high.
Those were all his words.
The next gentleman agreed with all his learned
friend had said. He suggested a form of punish-
ment which, under similar circumstances, has
suggested itself to men of many nations, and
waited. An older chief did not think the mode
severe enough. What did the others think?
They left it in the hands of the chief whose honor
had been injured. Would Te Parehao speak?
Yes ; he wanted the life of the white man and
his mistress that night. As for the kind of
punishment proposed—no, Te Parehao would
eooner butcher the white man outright. Plenty
of supplejacks he would take to lash the two
together ; and he wanted help to drag them to the
coast. The sea was big—the sea might wash out
their stain. First he would go to the house of
the white man, and tie him up; then away to
where the woman was, and ask her to come home.
The rest would be easy. Who would help him ?
Two of the strongest offered their services at
once.
« » •
It was moonlight, and nine o’clock ; and
the premises of Joseph Mackerrow, storekeeper,
had been closed for two or three hours. The
owner himself had been out in the moonlight half-
an-hour ago, but he had gone in, and now there
was no lights in the place. No lights, and no one
about.
Hush ! Was not that a woman who walked
quickly up in the pale moonlight toward the door,
and opened it, and went in? And what of three
figures that lay in the shadow of a clump of
blackish-trunked manuka trees huddled together
near the house ? It was hard to see them, no
matter how you peered.
It must have been half-an-hour after the woman
went in when these figures got up and approached
the door noiselessly. It was unlocked, and in
they went without a sound, each carrying a bundle
of split supplejacks.
Then everything was inanimate again.
Time passed. The place was now in shadow,
now lit up, and again in shadow, as a few clouds
straggled across the moon. When at length all
was clear, and she looked down, the three chiefs
were out in front of the house once more; and on
the ground near them, a man and woman lay in
each other’s arms, lashed together in half-a-dozen
different places. They formed a picture that
seemed to please Te Parehao’s idea of revenge; for
he regarded them often, and occasionally even
spoke to the woman in Maori, to the man in
broken English.
His wife of four months shed tears, only to be
jeered at; and the gum-merchant was fast going
into a stupor—partly through fear, and partly
because the tight binding was beginning to affect
his circulation.
They made too heavy a load to drag, so their
captors decided upon securing the services of a
cart-horse. And then another delicate question
took a few minutes’ consideration. Te Parehao
wished to hook the traces in some loops of the
supplejack binding, and perform the journey
without a sledge. His companions remonstrated ;
he would have no satisfaction when he reached the
coast. The way was rough, and it would kill
them. Te Parehao gave in reluctantly. When
all was ready to start, he stated his intention of
doing the rest of the work alone; rubbed noses
with his two assistants, and started on his way
merry as a chief should be who is enjoying a
thoroughly satisfactory revenge.
The moon had done three - fourths of her
night’s journey when he reached the coast, and
the strong salt air came pleasantly up over the
cliffs into his nostrils. He estimated the number
of capsizes on the way to be three. He never
looked back; but he heard the thud when the
sledge struck anything and turned over, and the
groans mingling with the traces’ rattle when the
load possibly caught against a stump, or helped
the sledge over a boulder. Then, when the horse
became quieter, he guessed the sledge had got
into its proper position again.
At the commencement of a steep incline, that
led down to a steeper cliff, Te Parehao stopped.
He walked to the very edge and looked coolly
over.
“ Good enough,” he said; and came back to
relieve the sledge of its load.
Then he uttered an exclamation. Either the
supplejacks binding the two necks had worked
loose, or the man’s neck was broken, for the head
had slipped down and rested on the shoulder of
the woman, whose eyes looked up now —not into
her lover’s, but into her husband’s. There was
still life in her.
‘‘Spare me,” she pleaded weakly, in the
native tongue. ‘‘The man you would kill with
me is already dead. Let me go ; and forget that
you married me. I will go away; only let me
live—let me live until my turn comes to die.”
For answer, Te Parehao lifted her partner s
head and placed the face against hers.”
“Kish ! ” he said ; “one good long kish.”
She did not speak, but when the head slipped
to the side again, her eyes turned on Te Parehao
with reproach, and the lower part of her face was
smeared with blood —blood not her own.
“Here! here!” said the chief. “Sure you no
sham ?” and he turned the pakeha’s face up.
Sham or no sham, one eye was glassy; and
where the other should have been there was a
simple hole. Blood ran from the mouth, and
clotted hlood was all over the beard.
“Ha! Never mind!” and Te Perehao pro-
ceeded to undo the fastenings that bound the man
and woman to the sledge. It did not take long.
“ See !” and he pointed down the sloping bank,
200 feet helow the edge of which lay the sea, glisten-
ing invitingly under the moon ; “ Quick way you
go to hell, eh ? Me gi’ you good shtart ’’—and he
began rolling them over. Five or six turns
sufficed; then they kept on without assistance,
and he stood and watched the result. Presently
the pace became hot; and first the woman, then the
man, was uppermost, whirling over and over and
hurrying to the edge.
# * *
It was days afterwards, and the sea rolled
in lazily. On the sand, a flock of birds of all sorts
and conditions; in the water, sometimes out of
sight, sometimes almost wholly to be seen, as each
wave curled over, something which interested
these birds greatly. A mermaid floating on her
back with her lover in her arms? Perhaps so.
At any rate, an old and dissipated-looking bird,
watching with the crowd, turned to his better half
and winked ; at which she looked the other way,
as a girl who blushes modestly. Yet, when the
waves had done with the two bodies, she was
among the first to waddle hungrily for a closer
inspection.

Going West.
[FOR T HE BULLENTIN.]
So you’ve left the town behind you
And in future we will find you
Out beyond the Never Never, out about the
boundless plain;
And I’m sorry that you’re going
Where the Western winds are blowing—
I was there when cheques were bigger, and I’ll
never go again.
For the roaring days are over,
And each unregenerate rover
Who had cheques to buy him welcome, and who
never lacked a friend,
Hardly earns a pound to squander,
But in search of work must wander
On an unrewarded journey that may never know
an end.
I was there when summer weather
And the Western grog together
Seemed to honeycomb my liver, so I gave the
whisky best;
And the relics of our party
May be living hale and hearty,
But I think you’ll find them sober if you meet
them in the West.
There is Tom, the Barcoo rambler,
And his mate, the two-up gambler;
Last I heard of them was somewhere down the
Flinders on the track ;
You will hear of Brown, the sawyer,
And his mate the back-blocks lawyer,
And some chaps I don’t remember who were
mates with Greenhide Jack;
But it doesn’t greatly matter
For they’ve mostly had to scatter,
And a lot of them took other names and rambled
further back,
Such as Scotty Mac, the ringer,
Likewise Sydney Bob, the singer ;
And, when you cross the Speewah, mind you ask
for Crooked Mick :
I am sure they’ll all befriend you.
And I’d gladly recommend you,
If you want a cheerful partner, just go mates with
Whistling Dick.
If you meet the Tambo Spider,
Or young Bricky Holt, the rider,
Ask them where Gilhooley’s got to—he].went out
with Lengthy Ike.
They’re all men of grit and daring,
Strong rough men of honest bearing,
Though they’ve grown a bit suspicious of all
strangers since the strike.
When your search for work is fruitless
And you wander lame and bootless,
When the summer drought is deadly, or in
winter’s dreary rain—
Blighted land and hellish weather,
You will curse the lot together—
I was there when cheques were bigger, and I’ll
never go again.
